T I T L E 
D A N I S H  T I T L E
Udvikling af Metodesamling til Human-Robot Interaction Design
E N G L I S H  T I T L E
Development of Toolbox Containing Methods for Human-Robot Interaction Design
D E S C R I P T I O N
This project aims to bridge the gab between knowledge obtained in Human-Robot Interaction research and the 
practice of developing robots in industry. The approach has been to map current research as well as development 
processes in Danish robotic companies to be able to identify specific needs and opportunities for new Human-Robot 
Interaction design methods. Several methods were developed through workshops with students and professionals 
and collected in a cohesive toolbox describing the useflow and purpose.
METTE BAUNBÆK PEDERSEN
Design and Innovation, DTU
KIRA MADSEN LORENZEN
Design and Innovation, DTU
PREFACE
==PAGE:3==
I I   |  
P R E FA C E
A B S T R A C T
The development and implementation of robotic products is on the rise on account of advancement in 
computational and sensory technology, but there is a lack of Human-Robot Interaction (HRI) design methodology 
in literature and practice. During this project it was seen that a lot of the knowledge obtained in academia in this 
area has not yet been utilized in industry in the development of robots. This report describes the development 
of a toolbox containing methods addressing the need to understand the perspective of the user in HRI 
development, fitting robotic products to their context, creating holistic HRI concepts, understanding and employing 
anthropomorphic elements, making cheaper and faster tests of interaction concepts as well as understanding users 
expectations to robotic products, as these were some of the main problem areas identified. The HRI Toolbox was 
designed to be used by a design team during the development phase of a project. The toolbox and methods were 
tested during three workshops with students and professionals and seen to be useful and implementable in the 
design process in several companies. In its final form the HRI Toolbox consists of the four methods:
•	
INTERACTION MAPPING - Getting a clear overview of the interactions of a robot and the steps involved.
•	
ROBOT PERSONALITY - Developing a personality for a robot to create a holistic character and interest in long-
term interactions by users.
•	
CONTEXT OF THE ROBOT- Being inspired from the sound, movement and appearance context to create 
concepts meeting the expectations of users.
•	
SCENARIO - Role-playing the expected interaction scenarios to evaluate them before building an expensive 
prototype.
The methods are to be used either in succession or hand picked to address the specific needs of the developers, 
in addition to other design and development methods. They can help in creating a more systematic and structured 
process, leading to faster development and fewer mistakes.
==PAGE:4==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   I I I
R E A D I N G  I N S T R U C T I O N S
The report is structured so that it can be read from beginning to end to gain full insight in the studies, process and 
literature behind the method development or a chapter at the time in correlation with the degree of background 
knowledge needed. Chapter 6 is a description of the final product and can be read separately to get to know the 
outcome of the project or to use directly in a Human-Robot Interaction design process. Throughout the report there 
are references to appendices that can be found in the back of this report.
N O M E N C L A T U R E
A N D R O I D 
An android is a robot that is build to resemble a human 
aesthetically. It mimics a human in appearance and 
behaviour and often has a flesh-like resemblance. An 
example of such a robot is Geminoid-DK by the Danish 
Professor Henrik Scharfe, created to be a copy of 
himself1.
A N T H R O P O M O R P H I C 
Anthropomorphism is a term used about the attribution 
of human trades, this be physical features or social 
features like emotions or behaviour. In relation to 
robotics this term can be used from very subtle 
elements such as resembling eyes with the use of two 
cameras to giving a robot a more human appearance.
D R O I D 
Short for android.
H O L I S T I C  D E S I G N 
When we speak of holistic design in this thesis the 
meaning is that of an HRI design, where all the parts 
are closely interconnected to form a unified whole. This 
covers all dimensions and behaviour of a robot.
1  Pictures and additional information about Geminoid-DK can be found 
at https://henrikscharfe.com/
H U M A N O I D  R O B O T 
Term for a robot with the shape of a human. Generally a 
humanoid robot has a torso, two arms, two legs and a 
head, but can also feature just part of the human shape 
e.g. from the torso up or head and facial features.
M U LT I M O D A L I T Y 
In this thesis multimodality is meant in the sense of 
being able to utilize several forms of inputs and outputs 
in communication e.g. visual, auditory, etc..
P E R C E I V E D  A F F O R D A N C E 
Term invented by the psychologist J. J. Gibson in 1977 
to explain the action a person expects they can do 
with/to an object, e.g. a regular chair looks intended 
for sitting. Perceived affordance can be, but is not 
necessarily the same as the real affordance. This is 
often where miscommunication happens which can lead 
to a worsened user experience and the lack of use of 
functions.
S E R V I C E  R O B O T S 
A service robot is one that performs useful tasks for 
humans or equipment, but does not include robots in an 
industrial automation application.
==PAGE:5==
I V   |  P R E FA C E
S O C I A L  R O B O T S 
A social robot is one that autonomously interacts with 
humans and other physical actors following social 
behaviours and rules in communicating.
U N C A N N Y  V A L L E Y 
Hypothesis described by the robotics professor 
Masahiro Mori in 1970. The hypothesis says that as 
robots approach human-like appearance and motion, 
humans will experience an increased familiarity and 
liking of the robot, until a certain point where this will 
drop and turn into an uncanny feeling and disliking. 
The hypothesis says that the uncanny feeling can be 
overcome by increasing the human-likeness further, 
leaving the uncanny valley, when you can no longer 
tell the difference between man and machine. The 
hypothesis is generally acknowledged in the field of 
Human-Robot Interaction and is constantly challenged 
and tested.
Z O O M O R P H I C 
Just like anthropomorphism is used to describe human-
like features in something, zoomorphism is used to 
describe any animal-like features. This is seen in e.g. the 
robotic dog Aibo by Sony2 or iCat by Philips Research3.
A B B R E V I A T I O N S
HRI 	
Human-Robot Interaction 
HCI 	
Human-Computer Interaction 
HHI 	
Human-Human Interaction 
GUI 	
Graphical User Interface
NUI 	
Natural User Interface
2  Pictures and additional information on Aibo can be found at http://
www.sony-aibo.com/
3  Pictures and additional information on iCat can be found at http://
www.hitech-projects.com/icat/

A P P R E C I A T I O N S
This report is based on interviews with experts in motion, sound and appearance as well as developers of robotic 
products within Danish companies. We would very much like to thank the motion, sound and appearance experts 
Anne Katrine Friis, Jakob Brahe-Pedersen, Jakob Welner, Jesper Ankarfeldt, Karsten Dines, Martina Pagura, Sebastian 
Cordes for their creative insights and inspiration. Furthermore we would like to give thanks to the robotic developers 
Jeppe Pedersen, Lars Dalgaard, Lasse Thomsen, Malene Tofveson Nibe, Niels Jul Jacobsen and Thomas Rubæk for 
there time and valuable inputs.
In the development of the HRI Toolbox we held two workshops with students. Thank you to the participants, Carl 
Vinggaard Kjeldsen, Christian Hoff, Christoffer Vincent Westh Thon, Emilie Munk Haagerup, Gustav Nordahl Jacobsen, 
Jakob Welner, Joen Rommedahl, Mads Lund Pedersen, Mads Rømer Svendsen, Marina Leisner Ejlertsen, Pernille 
Randrup Vergmann, Søren Boesen and Yingchao Xiong for their interest in the subject as well as time and feedback 
resulting in the methods seen in chapter 6.
This report wouldn’t have had the insights to the current scene of robot development without the elaborative 
comments from the workshop with employees at Blue Ocean Robotics, LT Automation, MiR and Teknologisk Institut. 
So thank you very much to Franziska Kirstein, Malene Tofveson Nibe, Niels Jul Jacobsen, Søren Peter Johansen and 
Thomas Rubæk for your time and participation in the discussion.
A thanks must also be given to Moises Pacheco from the DTU Electrical Engineering for the discussion on 
integration of playware technologies, to Elizabeth Ann Jochum from Robotic Aesthetic at AAU for the interview 
containing many insights to the current scene of the academic development of robotics, and to Jaap Daalhuizen 
from DTU Management for his constructive criticism on the final methods and help within the staging and useflow of 
the toolbox.
Lastly we would like to thank Philip Cash for his continuous guidance, critique, corrections and untiring enthusiasm 
that is reflected all through this project.

L E A R N I N G  O B J E C T I V E S
1.	
Attain a good understanding of - and be able to reflect on - relevant scientific and engineering knowledge 
within the design of HRI based on research literature
2.	
Be able to identify relevant scientific problems within design of HRI and be able to formulate clear and 
relevant research questions 
3.	
Be able to identify and review the relevant scientific literature and based on a literature review be able to 
develop a conceptual framework guiding the empirical work
4.	
Be able to assess, select and apply appropriate scientific methods, tools and competencies during the 
project
5.	
Be able to assess, select and apply appropriate design methods, tools and competencies to the project 
problem
6.	
Be able to analyse explain and discuss relevant professional and scientific problems
7.	
Be able to manage work and development situations that are complex and unforeseen and require new 
solution models
8.	
Be able to independently initiate and carry out discipline-specific and crossdisciplinary cooperation and to 
assume professional responsibility
9.	
Be able to disseminate research-based knowledge as well as the final design approach and outcome
10.	 Be able to set up a clear framework for the thesis and communicate the work clearly and in an 
understandable manner
11.	
Be able to provide proper documentation, including proper referencing throughout the project and provide a 
complete reference list
12.	 Adjust the staging of the project based on an assessment of the magnitude of the task, complexity and 
desired result1 
1  This learning objective will be adressed in the accompanying deliverable ”Reflection of Project Plan”.

INTRODUCTION TO THE SUBJECT 	 4
==PAGE:16==
The robots are coming! According to the observations 
of Moore’s Law (see figure 1–1A) the computational 
power is doubling every 18th month and this 
exponential growth is said to extend to all development 
of technology. Today it is possible to create 
computational power that is able to do the same 
amount of calculations per second as a mouse brain 
at the cost of only $1.000 (see figure 1–1B). If the trend 
continues the computational power of a human brain 
will be reached around year 2025 and at a fraction of 
the cost 10 years from that (Kurzweil 2005). This means 
that robots will soon be cheap and intelligent enough 
to be deployed in a great variety of functions. The 
global market for robotics is also growing as foreseen. 
I N  T H E  F O L LO W I N G  C H A P T E R  W E  W I L L  G I V E  A N  I N T R O D U C T I O N  T O  T H E 
P R O J E C T.  T H E  R E A S O N S  B E H I N D  I T  W I L L  B E  P R E S E N T E D  A N D  T H E  A I M 
D E F I N E D .  I N  A D D I T I O N  W E  W I L L  S H A R E  T H E  R E A S E A R C H  Q U E ST I O N  A N D 
O B J E C T I V E S  T H AT  H AV E  L E A D  T H E  P R O J E C T.  I N  T H E  E N D  T H E  ST R U C T U R E  O F 
T H E  R E P O R T  W I L L  B E  E X P L A I N E D  A N D  A N  I N T R O D U C T I O N  T O  T H E  S U B J E C T 
W I L L  B E  G I V E N .
INTRODUCTION
figure 1–1A.  MOORE’S LAW
figure 1–1B.  EXPONENTIAL GROWTH
figure 1–1.	 THE SPEED OF TECHNOLOGICAL DEVELOPMENT SHOWN IN MOORE’S LAW AND THE EXPO- NENTIAL GROWTH OF COMPUTING 
(MOORE’S LAW - THE FIFTH PARADIGM N.D., EXPONENTIAL GROWTH OF COMPUTING N.D.).
==PAGE:17==
2   |  
I N T R O D U C T I O N
Today the robotics industry has a global market outlook 
of approximately $50 billion. That number is predicted 
to double within the next 3 years, with service robots 
as the fastest growing section (International Federation 
of Robotics 2015). Bill Gates wrote an article in 2007 
predicting “...a future in which robotic devices will 
become a nearly ubiquitous part of our day-to-day 
lives.” (Gates 2007) and if we look at Gartner’s Hype 
Cycle of Emerging Technologies1 for 2015 smart robots 
are climbing the first Peak of Expectations and are 
predicted to reach the Plateau of Productivity in 5 - 10 
years (see figure 1–2) (Gartner 2015). The technological 
development is progressing rapidly which means that 
1  Gartner’s Hype Cycle of Emerging Technologies is a model of 
predictions for the domestication of up and coming technologies. The 
technologies follow the stages: Innovation Trigger, Peak of Inflated 
Expectations, Trough of Disillusionment, Slope of Enlightenment 
and finally Plateau of Productivity where it is seen as an accepted 
technology.
robots are a part of our world now, and are most likely 
going to be a part of our future, but how they should be 
designed is still up for discussion. 
Our technical capabilities are not the only thing holding 
us back from implementing robots on a broader 
scale. According to a large survey by the European 
Union in 2012, 60% of EU citizens think that robots 
should be banned from caring for children, elderly and 
the disabled and 70% agree that robots could steal 
people’s jobs (TNS Opinion & Social 2012). This shows 
a general scepticism in the European public about the 
use of robots, especially in social situations. The only 
experience many people have with robots stem from 
violent films featuring killer robots, taking over the 
world. It is not surprising that they are hesitant to bring 
them into their homes (Katz, Halpern & Crocker 2015). 
figure 1–2.	GARTNER’S HYPE CYCLE FOR 2015 (GARTNER 2015).
==PAGE:18==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   3
At the same time we are looking towards the largest 
human population in history, and because we live longer, 
we have a vast amount of elderly people needing care 
in their daily lives (Pettinger 2013). This is a paradigm 
shift and a majority of robot developers believe that we 
look towards a future, where the solution to the growing 
social sector is managed by robots (e.g. Saez-Pons, 
Lehmann, Syrdal & Dautenhahn 2014, Pollack, Engberg, 
Matthews, Dunbar-jacob, Mccarthy & Thrun 2002, Gee, 
Browne & Kawamura 2005). The humans that share the 
working space of the robots need to feel comfortable 
with their new partners and accept their presence for 
this to be successful. Such a successful user experience 
originates in the interactions between human and 
machine, which we believe needs to be designed with 
equal care as the functional aspects. 
We see a gap in the research on robotic design, where 
designers need new tools dedicated to the development 
of robots to be able to consistently produce good 
results in user experience.
1 . 1   A I M  O F  P R O J E C T
The aim of the project is to improve the user 
experience of social and service robots by providing 
a Human-Robot Interaction design toolbox that can 
support designers and engineers in structuring their 
development process.
The focus of the toolbox is threefold. Where other 
research have focused on one part of the interaction, 
we aim at providing a range of methods that will 
consider the multi-modality of robots to create a 
holistic solution. Secondly, the work is focused towards 
proximate interaction with robots needing to work in 
an environment shared with humans and the user 
experience when interacting with them. Finally the 
methods take leave in the technical possibilities of the 
present and near future, where the uncanny valley is 
difficult to surpass and robots are more likely to have 
one or a few simple tasks rather than serving multiple 
purposes.
1 . 2   R E S E A R C H 
Q U E S T I O N S
With this project we aim to answer the following 
research question:
How can the current practice of HRI design be 
improved to create better user experiences with 
robots?
To answer this we have the following objectives:
•	
What are the factors influencing user experience 
in HRI?
•	
What methods are currently being used in HRI 
design and how is the process structured?
•	
In which areas are the learnings from current 
HRI research unaccounted for in practice?
•	
What methods are currently being used to 
design sound, movement and visual appearance 
outside the field of robotics and how can they 
inspire new methods for HRI
•	
What methods can we develop to support the 
utilization of learnings from current HRI research 
in practice?
•	
How can these methods be implemented in the 
current development practice of robots?
1 . 3   S T R U C T U R E  O F 
R E P O R T
The report begins with a short introduction to the 
project and subjects of robotics and interactions in 
==PAGE:19==
4   |  
chapter 1. This is followed by the results from an 
extensive literature review within the general factors 
in Human-Robot Interaction, the main dimension 
and current design methods, tools and frameworks 
in literature in chapter 2. Next the initial studies, 
chapter 4, take departure in 13 interviews with robot 
developers and motion, sound and appearance 
experts to investigate current practice. This lead up 
to an exploratory study, chapter 5, which uses three 
workshops as basis for the development of methods 
and a toolbox for HRI design. In chapter 6 the final 
design of the HRI Toolbox and methods are presented 
together with their suggested useflow, implications, 
limitations and alternatives. Lastly the report goes 
through a discussion of the relation and implications for 
literature and the robotic industry in chapter 7. Finally, 
a conclusion and the future work with the project is 
presented in chapter 8 and 9. Attached to the report is 
an appendix containing interview notes, worksheets on 
the development of the methods, an idea catalogue of 
8 methods from which the final concepts were chosen 
and the schedules from the workshops. Some of the 
figures and method templates can also be found in the 
appendix in bigger size for a better reading experience.
1 . 4   I N T R O D U C T I O N  T O 
T H E  S U B J E C T
The following is a short introduction to the subject of 
robotics and interaction. We will define what a robot 
is and describe the developments within the field on 
a broader scale. To gain a better understanding of 
the field of interaction we will dig deeper into social 
sciences understanding of Human-Human Interaction, 
where the resemblance to Human-Computer and 
Human-Robot Interaction lies and where to be aware of 
changes in implications.
1 . 4 . 1   I N T R O D U C T I O N  T O 
R O B O T I C S
When you mention the word robot to anyone not 
working with development in the field, images of metal 
human shaped machines with diodes and voltmeters 
often comes to mind. The image of a robot is influenced 
by movies, games and literature from the last 100 years 
trying to prepare us for, what a robot should be able to 
do. In a study made by Höflich & El Bayed (2015), the 
interviewees were asked to draw, what they thought of, 
when hearing the word ”robot”. The result drew more 
resemblance to characters like C-3PO and R2-D2 from 
Star Wars than functioning robots from modern times. 
This shows the direct influence of expectations created 
by media.
The use of the term robot was first published in 1921 
by Karel Čapek and can be translated to ”slave” or 
”forced labour” from the Czech term robota. The Oxford 
Dictionary define a robot as
”A machine capable of carrying out a complex series 
of actions automatically especially one programmable 
by a computer.” 
(OXFORD DICTIONARY N.D.)
The limits of this definition are vague and in many 
cases it can be discussed whether or not a product falls 
within the category of being a robot. The definition does 
not talk about the ability to physically move. From this 
description any computer product could be classified 
as a robot, but it is generally not seen that way (Allen 
2012). On the other hand many moving everyday 
products have computers incorporated these days, but 
automatic doors, elevators and a sewing machines with 
digital displays are generally not identified as robots 
either. The degree and nature of the complexity needed 
for a machine to qualify as a robot is not well-defined. 
I N T R O D U C T I O N
==PAGE:20==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   5
We have therefore come up with our own definition 
of a robot for a better understanding of the intended 
meaning throughout this report. We define a robot as 
a moving physical machine controlled by a computer 
that is able to act autonomously to some degree on 
sensory input from its environment.
D E V E L O P M E N T S  I N  R O B O T I C S
Robots have existed in industry as a device of 
automation since 1962 (RobotWorx 2015) (see figure 1–3) 
and sales have grown so that within the last year they 
have increased by 29% to 229,261 units (International 
Federation of Robotics 2015). Especially the car 
industry is well known for having utilised robotic arms 
in various sizes in its production. Most of these have 
been expensive, heavy, rigid and working behind safety 
fences, but recently a new trend is emerging employing 
cheaper, lighter and more flexible robots, that can share 
their workspace with humans without the risk of injuries. 
An example of this is robots produced by the company 
Universal Robots (Solon 2015).
Outside of industrial automation a big part of research 
have gone from focussing on mobile platform robots 
(like Shakey, Stanfords first mobile robot from 1972, 
see figure 1–3) to focus more on humanoid robots. One 
of the forces behind this direction in the beginning of 
the 21 century, was the DARPA organisation, massively 
funded by the American Military. Their main priority has 
been to develop robots, who can take over assignments 
for humans in situations, where the jobs are too dull, 
dirty or dangerous. An example is in search and rescue 
missions, where the flexibility of a human is needed 
to overcome e.g. difficult terrain, open doors or use 
different tools, but the environment is too dangerous 
for humans to work in. This has resulted in considerable 
advances in the research on humanoid robots and 
created a leap to the development of social and services 
robots, with specific examples like the Centibots Project 
from 2002 and Atlas, by Boston Dynamics in 2014 
(figure 1–3).
The other side to the humanoid development lies in 
a curiosity about the human condition, how we act, 
understand our surroundings and express our selves. 
With a point of origin in very human-like robots, e.g. 
Geminoid DK from 2002 (see figure 1–3), researchers 
have sought to investigate, where the border of 
humanity lies and how the human intelligence can be 
copied and maybe eventually build upon. Social robots 
have a base in the investigation of the social intelligence 
and frontrunners like Cynthia Breazeal (creator of Jibo, 
2016) have a vision of everybody having personal robots 
in their homes. 
Predecessors to this are robotic toys that have had an 
ongoing progress as well, from FRED developed in 1982, 
to Furby and Aibo from 1998 and 1999 respectively (see 
figure 1–3). These have inspired socially intelligent robot 
design to help with challenges in child learning and 
development as well as resulting in social robots like 
Pepper and companion robots for elderly suffering from 
dementia or loneliness, like Paro from 1993, still very 
much in use. 
Personal drones have also been on the rise due to 
reduced component costs and increased competition. 
According to Holden (2015) around 4 million consumer 
drones was predicted to be sold within the year 2015, 
rising to 16 million by 2020. Many ordinary products are 
also seeing increased automation, from smart vacuum 
cleaners like the Roomba from 2002, to inteligent 
laundry machines and driver-less cars - the robotic 
revolution is creeping in everywhere.
==PAGE:21==
6   |  
I N T R O D U C T I O N
figure 1–3.	ROBOTIC DEVELOPMENT THROUGHOUT HISTORY.
==PAGE:22==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   7
1 . 4 . 2   I N T R O D U C T I O N  T O 
I N T E R A C T I O N
To explain the concept of interaction we look towards 
one of the greatly recognized sociologists, Erving 
Goffman, who have defined the parameters and patterns 
involved in Human-Human Interaction (HHI). This is 
done to inform the understanding of Human-Computer 
Interaction (HCI) and Human-Robot Interaction (HRI) and 
show the key similarities and differences.
Goffman created the concept of face-to-face interaction 
that seek to describe an interaction between two human 
beings (Goffman 1955). Goffman looks at two people 
ready to engage in some sort of interaction with each 
other, this may be intentional on both parts or just on 
one. He uses different terms to describe the actors’ 
expectations and understanding of the situation. He 
explains that both human beings have a true self, a line, 
a social symbol and a face.
Their true self can be understood as their personality. 
It is how they are in their core. This implicit lies behind 
their choices and their own understanding of their 
personality. The line is how they view and portray 
themselves and this determines the expectations they 
have to their own behaviour during interactions. The line 
is the front a person puts up to deal with an interaction 
in a specific situation. This can change according to the 
situation or company of the person, e.g. the line might 
be different if a person is talking to friends, family or co-
workers. The face on the other hand is the way a person 
is viewed by others during any given situation. It could 
be that a person is viewed as helpful or idiotic during an 
interaction. A persons social symbol is the way they are 
seen initially by others and the way they are introduced. 
This is upheld both by how they see themselves (the 
line) and how others view them (the face). It could be 
on account of their social status or profession, which 
make people portray a different line towards them - act 
differently according to their own status. See figure 1–4 
for an illustration of the terms.
Goffman argues that the first part of any interaction 
is an action, that comes as a set of movements with 
a meaning or purpose from the first person. When 
directed towards another human being this is defined 
as social actions or behaviours. This implies the hope of 
some sort of response from the counter part to make it 
a successful interaction, may this be a new action or a 
reaction that indicates e.g. understanding, repulsion or 
consensus. From that point there is either a continuous 
action-reaction or a separation with the hope of face-
preservation and agreed understanding of the initial 
objective of the interaction. 
Goffman (1955) describes that each actor have some 
expectations to the interaction, which is important to 
understand to achieve a successful outcome. Every 
interaction is handled with impression management 
from each of the individuals both knowingly (the line 
and social status) and unwittingly (true self and face) to 
achieve individual goals.
”When an individual projects a definition of the 
situation and thereby makes an implicit or explicit 
claim to be a person of a particular kind, he 
automatically exerts a moral demand upon the others, 
obliging them to value and treat him in the manner 
that persons of his kind have a right to expect.” 
- GOFFMAN, 1958
For the sake of the current context, where we focus on 
positive user experiences with robots, we would like to 
define, what we believe to be a successful interaction. 
We define it as a situation, where the expectations of 
both actors are met, or reveals as a positive surprise, 
the objectives are understood and the reaction follows 
==PAGE:23==
8   |  
as a natural occurrence to the initial action. Thereby 
the objective is fulfilled and both parties separate with 
their face upheld. This is in line with Goffman’s (1983) 
descriptions of positive interaction experiences.
The theory by Goffman is widely accepted in sociology 
(Jacobsen 2015, Schein, Hughes, Dalton & Goffman 
1989) and also used to explain scenarios far beyond 
HHI. Humphreys (2005) suggest that people map their 
understanding of common social rules and dilemmas 
onto new technologies based on Goffman’s theory. 
She argues that the disturbance of a ringing cellphone 
is demanding the same reaction from a user, as if 
an outside person had interrupted, why the social 
interaction with a person or with only the representation 
of a person, the cellphone, results in the same social 
scenario.
H U M A N - C O M P U T E R  I N T E R A C T I O N
When looking at the development of technology, the 
introduction of computers was a disruption of the direct 
HHI. In this sense HCI can be seen as a step on the way 
towards HRI. It is often the source of inspiration when 
designing interactions with robots since the actors in 
the scenario is similar; Man, machine and environment 
(Sucar, Aviles & Miranda-Palma 2003). The field of 
HCI is more widely researched (Fernaeus, Ljungblad, 
Jacobsson & Taylor 2009, Hoffman & Ju 2014) than the 
field of HRI, mainly because of it’s earlier origination. 
I N T R O D U C T I O N
figure 1–4.	VISUALISATION OF ERVING GOFFMAN’S THEORY ABOUT SOCIAL INTERACTION.
==PAGE:24==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   9
HCI has developed over decades and started out with 
being designed only for professionals. The development 
of the graphical user interface (GUI) changed the way 
humans interacted with computers and provided the 
bigger population with access to the computational 
hardware without a programmer’s skill set (Whittaker 
2013). Relating this to Goffman’s terms, you can say 
that the line of the computer changed with the new 
interface, while the social symbol changed gradually 
hereafter, as it made it possible to use the computer for 
private purposes. 
While the GUI provided a big part of the population 
with the ability to interact with computers, the abstract 
concept of using a computer mouse or understand a 
folder structure was still tricky for many, especially from 
the older generations. A new development of the GUI 
concept has been termed Natural User Interface (NUI) 
and aspire to implement a naturalistic and intuitive 
understanding of how to interact with computer 
devices. An example of this is the interaction with tablet 
computers, where a touch interface is used instead of a 
mouse, leaving the user to directly point to the program 
or folder of interest.
The interaction with computers can on some levels be 
compared to the interaction between humans. Just 
like Frohlich (1991) uses Goffman’s theory to underline 
the importance of knowing the interaction order when 
designing Human-Computer Interfaces, the presented 
terminology on HHI by Goffman can provide a way of 
understanding the development of today’s HCI. When 
humans interact with a computer, they are doing so 
on the foundation of implicit and explicit expectations 
(Wesson, Winckler, Part & Hutchison 2013), which 
is similar to Goffman’s use of the terms impression 
management and face preservation. 
Goffman did not see non-human entities as having 
a true self (Goffman 1959). He argued that they were 
only props utilized by humans and therefore excluded 
them from his studies on interaction. Cerulo (2009) on 
the other hand disagrees on this point. She describes 
how non-human entities can easily affect humans 
in interactions. When interacting with a computer 
e.g. people know that it is without intentions, but 
still subconsciously read a personality, status and 
expectations into the product.
H U M A N - R O B O T  I N T E R A C T I O N
Human-Robot Interaction (HRI) is a multidisciplinary 
field influenced by both HCI (Sucar et al. 2003) and 
social sciences (Frohlich 1991) and mainly pushed by 
technology’s progression. Goodrich & Schultz (2007) 
divides robotic interaction into two general categories 
depending on the proximity of the interacting agents: 
Remote interaction and proximate interaction. In the 
first category the human and robot are not located 
in the same place. Here the interaction is a form of 
teleoperation or remote controlling similar to HCI. In the 
second category the human and robot are co-located 
making physical interaction possible. In this category 
NUIs have been popular in research, featuring e.g.  
communication through spoken commands.
Just like the introduction of the GUI disrupted the HCI, 
the new dimension of the autonomous mobile physical 
presence of a computational product changes the way 
humans interact with robots, compared to computers 
(Fernaeus et al. 2009). As the line of robots to a larger 
degree resembles the autonomous actors humans 
usually interact with, they portray similar expectations to 
the machine as towards another human (Young, Sung, 
Voida, Sharlin, Igarashi, Christensen & Grinter 2011).
==PAGE:25==
1 0   |  
”The way people interact with robots appears to be 
fundamentally different from how they interact with 
most other technologies. People tend to ascribe a 
level of intelligence and sociability to robots that 
influences their perception of how the interactions 
should proceed. If a robot, for its part, adheres to this 
ascription, then the interactions tend to be natural 
and engaging; if not, they can be discordant.” 
- SIMMONS, MAKATCHEV, KIRBY, LEE, FANASWALA, BROWNING, 
FORLIZZI & SAKR 2011
Looking at HRI through the perspective of Goffman’s 
theory on HHI, Paepcke & Takayama (2010) argues 
that the face applied to robots by humans is currently 
influenced by science fiction, which often portrays a 
dooms day scenario with robots as the enemy. The 
robots’ social symbol can be based on the company 
they are produced by or the country it is produced in, 
but is also derived from appearance and behaviour. 
If they e.g. have a visual appearance resembling a 
dog, they are treated differently than if they had the 
appearance of a grown man (Fong et al. 2003).
On the other hand it is difficult to talk about the true 
self of a robot. Most robots are programmed to function 
in different tasks, but are seldom provided with a 
personality, as it does not have any apparent value. 
Simmons et al. (2011) seems to disagree on this point. 
In their research they have given their roboceptionist 
an elaborate backstory to keep the users interested in 
the robot. Their view is that giving robots more depth 
will improve long term interaction. This is supported 
by Kanda, Hirano, Eaton & Ishiguro (2004), who 
experienced a drop in interest in interacting with their 
robot Robovie after one week. They suggest giving 
robots the ability to learn more about their users and 
form a closer personalized relationship over time to 
increase interest in long-term interaction. An very 
successful attempt of a long term interaction robot was 
seen in the late 90’s toy Furby (see figure 1–3), that by 
being programmed to start out speaking completely 
Furbish (a gibberish language all Furbys speak) after a 
while started using English words as to simmulate that 
the child was teaching it to speak (Breemen 2004). This 
made it popular enough to be redesigned and marketed 
first in 2005 and then again in 2012.
While the terms of face and line can be used about 
robots, they are slightly different than in HHI. The line 
and face of the robot does not usually change according 
to the context. A robot acts the same if it is interacting 
with a child or the queen. It does not (yet, anyway) 
react to the social symbols or lines of others. This may 
be one of the keys to improving HRI as it is in these 
situations, where humans’ expectations to interactions 
are not being met and they might loose face as a result.
What is important to take from the theory of Goffman 
is the layers of which an interaction is made up. When 
designing with this knowledge in the back of our head, 
we can by affecting the line and social status help 
to preserve the face of both the robot and user. This 
will make sure that not only is the current interaction 
a pleasurable one but also help to ensure continuous 
interest and thereby ongoing interactions. Another 
important take away is the importance in knowing the 
difference between computer interaction and robot 
interaction - the physical componant - and that it 
influences the user experience. Because of the new 
technology it will be hard to anticipate just how it will 
affect the users expectations, why user tests and quick 
agile iterations will be very important. 
I N T R O D U C T I O N
==PAGE:26==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   1 1

C O U L D  P R OV I D E  A  C O N T R I B U T I O N .
2 . 1   G E N E R A L  F A C T O R S 
I N  H U M A N - R O B O T 
I N T E R A C T I O N
Some characteristics of HRI are not tied to specific 
dimension of interaction, but are still highly influential 
on the overall perception of a robot. In this first section 
we present the research on a few general factors 
identified in the literature that has an impact on 
general user experience in HRI.
2 . 1 . 1   A  N E W  S P E C I E S
It is well accepted that humans ascribe human qualities 
to humanoid robots (Haring, Watanabe & Mougenot 
2013, Katz & Halpern 2014, Powers & Kiesler 2006, 
Fong, Nourbakhsh & Dautenhahn 2003) and therefore 
have a certain ”like-me” attitude towards them. 
It is also clear that when robots take on a certain 
well known look, as e.g. a dog (Fong et al. 2003), 
humans prescribe it dog-like features instead of 
general animalistic features (Haring et al. 2013). Duffy 
(2003) argues that the reason for ascribing robots 
anthropomorphic or zoomorphic features is two fold; 
to design systems that can function in our physical 
and social space and to take advantage of them as 
mechanisms through which social interaction with 
people can be facilitated. At the same time he points 
out that the first argument can easily be dismissed. 
The anthropomorphic robot is not the only one that 
can function in our environment. In many cases wheels 
are superior to the bipedal motion of imitating legs. 
Furthermore the computational nature of robots have 
different advantages than that of humans. So maybe 
they need to be thought of as a new species.
Robertson (2010) agrees on this point. Her research 
is focused on the genderfication of robots and how 
that reinforces gender stereotypes in society. She 
speculates that unconsciously many (male) robotisist 
create robots with female characteristics for typical 
female jobs and vice versa for robots with male 
characteristics. This might be on account of complying 
with users expectations, but could damage the cause 
of gender equality. She advices complete gender 
neutral robot designs to avoid this issue.
LITERATURE REVIEW
==PAGE:29==
1 4   |  L I T E R AT U R E  R E V I E W
The subject of anthropomorphic designs also raises 
some other ethical concerns. It is speculated whether 
inducing robots with emotional and social cues will 
provoke humans to protect them, beyond what is 
reasonable. Perhaps endangering themselves in the 
process (Novikova & Watts 2015). Yet another issue is 
identified with the widely recognized theory by Mori 
(2012); the Uncanny Valley. It predicts that robots, and 
especially moving robots, will cause an uneasy feeling 
in the humans interacting with them, if they looks too 
much like human beings.
It seems, based on this, that copying the human form 
meticulously, when designing robots is not a good idea, 
while including anthropomorphic elements are. The use 
of anthropomorphism and zoomorphism should be used 
with care as robots are an entirely new species and 
should be treated and designed as such. 
2 . 1 . 2   E X P E C T A T I O N 
M A N A G E M E N T
It is still fairly uncommon for most people to interact 
with robots in their daily lives, so many of the 
expectations created around robots originate from 
science fiction, be it books, comics or movies (Katz 
et al. 2015). In these stories the robots are often fully 
autonomous, able to speak, walk and blend in with 
humans in society, a long way away from current 
technological development. Interacting with a real robot 
for the first time might be a disappointing affair for 
many people and can result in discontinued use. If the 
goal is to create robots for the home DiSalvo, Gemperle, 
Forlizzi & Montgomery (2003) and Hoffman (2013) 
argues that it can be better to announce the robot as 
a robotic product to evoke a resemblance to consumer 
products, since a lot of the products will be closer to 
smart home appliances than robots seen on film and in 
Sci-Fi books.
Research has shown humans’ expectations of a 
robot’s performance to rise when they interact with 
a familiar looking robot, be it an anthropomorphic or 
zoomorphic agent (Paepcke & Takayama 2010). For 
this reason it might be worthwhile considering the 
degree of anthropomorphism applied to a robot and 
the reaction this might cause. Designing a robot with 
strong anthropomorphic features can result in higher 
expectations to the robots abilities. If they are not met, 
the user experience is flavoured with disappointment. 
In the study by Paepcke & Takayama (2010) a group 
of people were presented with a robot and asked to 
rate its competences. Half the people were told, that 
it was a very intelligent robot before interacting with it 
and the other half that it was not. The people whose 
expectations were set high at the beginning ended up 
rating its competences lower after interacting with it, 
than the people whose expectations were set low at the 
beginning. So to follow this parameter, it would be better 
to under-promise rather than over-sell the capabilities 
of a robot, if the goal is to avoid disappointment with 
the users.
To clearly communicate the true level of competences 
in robots can be a challenging affair. A complete list of 
technical specifications is of course a possibility, but for 
most consumers it will not tell a meaningful story. Many 
are uninterested in the technical details or neglect to 
read the manual. In order to manage users expectations 
it is necessary to design sounds, appearance and 
movement of the robot in such a way that it clearly 
communicates the affordance of the robot.
==PAGE:30==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   1 5
2 . 1 . 3   C U L T U R A L 
R E F E R E N C E S
As mentioned earlier the theory of acceptance of robots, 
Masahiro Mori’s Uncanny Valley was described in 1970 
and is widely agreed upon today (Mori 2012, Gee et al. 
2005, Brayda, Chellali, Brayda & Chellali 2012, Fong et 
al. 2003, Sofge 2010, Vincent, Taipale, Sapio, Lugano & 
Editors 2015). But even though it is accepted globally 
there are several factors including cultural references 
found to have an influence of the size of the valley.
Apart from media and pop-culture, a big influence on 
acceptance is the religious culture of the user (Gee et 
al. 2005). In the Japanese Shinto religion there is an 
acceptance of inanimate things having a soul, which 
makes it easier to accept and understand the human 
likeness in a robot. Other religions, such as Judeo-
Christianity, believe that increasing humanity in inhuman 
objects is a disrespectful way of “playing god” (Tabuchi 
2015, Katz et al. 2015). In a study by Katz et al. (2015) a 
questionnaire was distributed to 789 graduate students. 
The results showed that Judeo-Christian religion was 
negatively related to robot-liking and religiosity in 
general was positively related to robot-phobia.
Prior encounters with robots have a say as well. In the 
above mentioned study people with prior experiences 
with robots were more likely to accept robots in fields, 
where they had already encountered robots. This 
aligns well with the evolution of technology in general. 
Imagining robots in their daily lives is too big of a task, 
if the only experience people have had with them before 
are from dystopian Hollywood movies. Katz et al. (2015) 
however argue that the human species can adapt quite 
quickly to changes in the technological landscape and 
that experiments on responses to the uncanny valley 
might not hold true in the future.
It seems that the design of a robot from one set of 
cultural references, with certain social cues build in, 
might not work well all over the world. The culture 
surrounding the robot as an entity, but also the social 
norms are radically different in different parts of the 
world. This should be taken into consideration, when 
designing robots and it should not be expected that one 
size fits all.
2 . 2   T H E  D I M E N S I O N S 
O F  H U M A N - R O B O T 
I N T E R A C T I O N
The field of HRI is extensive, covering all the different 
interaction forms we humans are able to utilize and 
interpret in communicating with others. Where as 
computers are limited to visual and audible cues, robots 
can have a wider variety of tools at their disposal. When 
communicating a message, a robot has the advantage 
of communicating it through several channels at 
once, just like humans do, increasing the chance of 
understanding with the recipient (Gorostiza, Barber, 
Khamis, Malfaz, Pacheco, Rivas, Corrales, Delgado & 
Salichs 2006). DiSalvo et al. (2003) argues that a holistic 
approach to the design of these dimensions in robotics 
is needed for them to be able to communicate clearly 
during interactions.
The biggest focus points relating to the communication 
modalities of robots from current HRI research are 
motion, sound and appearance. Motion has been 
explored both as body and head movements, facial 
expressions as well as more technical parameters 
like speed, direction and animation. Sound has been 
investigated through different strategies, from the use 
of verbal and gibberish languages to non-linguistic 
utterances, changing technical parameters like pitch, 
frequency, rhythm and intonation to create a variety 
==PAGE:31==
1 6   |  
of emotional meanings. Finally appearance has been 
explored within the industrial design of the robot from 
the humanoid approaching the Uncanny Valley to 
functional objects such as a robotic vacuum cleaner.
As mentioned before these are not the only dimensions 
in HRI, but they currently act as the biggest influencers 
on the interaction between humans and robots. Other 
dimensions that could be interesting to include in a 
future study would be odour, feel and taste. For this 
project however we have decided to focus our attention 
to the three modalities motion, sound and appearance 
as they are the most well documented, but also because 
they are dimensions relevant to all robot designs. Not 
all robots are designed to be touched or tasted and it 
can be very difficult to communicate through odour, 
but from our previous definition of robots, they all need 
a physical appearance, they all have moving parts 
and they all feature some sounds, whether ubiquitous 
mechanical or intentional communicative sounds. In 
the following sections we will elaborate on the research 
findings within each of the main dimensions. 
2 . 2 . 1   M O T I O N
There is a general understanding in HRI research that 
movements have a significant impact on how humans 
perceive and understand a robots’ manufactured 
intentions (Hoffman & Ju 2014). The gaze, for example, 
works as an indicator for understanding and attention 
between humans, and this has proven usable in HRI 
as well (Hoffman & Ju 2014, Lehmann, Saez-Pons, 
Syrdal & Dautenhahn 2015, Saez-Pons et al. 2014). Eye 
contact or a stare towards something can either show 
understanding of speech or intention in behaviour.
Small social cues in movement (gazing, nodding, etc.) 
can create an understanding that a robot is more 
intelligent than if it did not move except if to solve 
a task (Lehmann et al. 2015), as these social motion 
cues, when used appropriately, are enough to generate 
a sense of action-ability (Terada, Shamoto, Mei & 
Ito 2007). To better embed this, general animation 
principles have be utilized in robot designs to make 
their reactions more lifelike, interesting and well-liked 
(Breemen 2004a). Many research groups refer to 
Disney’s 12 principles of animation as a toolset that can 
be used in designing robots’ movements (Johnston 
& Thomas 1995). The team behind the Jibo robot 
is one such example (Team Jibo 2015b). For others 
such as Jochum, Schultz, Johnson & Murphey (2014) 
puppeteering has been the inspiration for robot designs, 
discovering how puppeteers imitate, simplify and 
exaggerate motions to make puppets seem lifelike on 
stage.
A lot of movements are understood the same way when 
done by robots as well as by humans (Novikova & Watts 
2015), but it is not always the case. For example when 
a robot approaches a person directly from the front, it 
provokes a sense of insecurity and is seen as a threat 
(Bethel & Murphy 2008). This means that it is not always 
constructive to translate, what we see in HHI directly to 
HRI. 
When designing movements for a robot it is therefore 
often the aim to show the robot’s ”intentions”. Here 
Dragan et.al. argues it is important to be aware of the 
difference between legibility, if the robot is easy to read, 
and predictability, if the humans expectations are being 
met (Dragan, Lee & Srinivasa 2013). By incorporating 
intent of the robot into the movement design, it has 
been shown to generate a better understanding of the 
robot and its purpose in the setting (Szafir, Mutlu & Fong 
2015), and thereby create a better acceptance of it’s 
presence. 
L I T E R AT U R E  R E V I E W
==PAGE:32==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   1 7
When considering social motion in non-humanoid 
robots, it is important to think beyond facial expressions 
and arm gesture, as there are often fewer degrees of 
freedom (DOF) and manipulators such as eyes, hands, 
fingers and the like. In more function-oriented robots 
the cues up for interpretation are often expressed in 
how they execute their task. For the ”intentions” of such 
a robot to become clear there are fewer parameters to 
work with and they can only rely on social cues that do 
not depend on a human appearance. An example of this 
is the work by Ju & Takayama (2009), where people 
were seen to attribute the motion of a automatic door to 
human-like characteristics such as cognition and intent 
and relate to this beyond the simple programming of 
the motion. When people can convey human emotions 
onto a simple motion made by a door, it suggests that 
motions in general can be used more effectively to 
enhance user experience and robot acceptance (Höflich 
& El Bayed 2015).
2 . 2 . 2   S O U N D
There is a consensus that sound is a key 
communication tool in HRI and that it can benefit from 
anthropomorphic elements. As seen by Walters, Syrdal, 
Koay, Dautenhahn & te Boekhorst (2008) the use of 
different voices for a robot can for instance determine 
the comfortable distance to a robot. Using a human 
voice participants would approach a robot as a close 
personal friend, where as a mechanical voice would 
make them approach it robot as a stranger and no voice 
at all as a ”dead” object. 
There is a general understanding that the use of 
natural language is the ultimate solution for audio 
communication with robots, why much of the research 
is focused in this direction. However as experienced 
by Lee, Forlizzi, Rybski, Crabbe, Chung, Finkle, Glaser & 
Kiesler (2009) it is complicated to implement. Giving a 
robot a few pre-programmed sentences to speak and 
understand is often insufficient, as humans will often 
use a lot of words in different orders to answer simple 
questions or not answer the question audible at all.
Instead a few researchers have tried a different 
approach. Yilmazyildiz, Henderickx, Vanderborght, 
Verhelst, Soetens & Lefeber (2011) have experimented 
with an emotional gibberish language that conveys the 
emotional state of the robot, but through words with 
no apparent meaning. By removing the meaning of the 
words expressed, the focus of the listener is on the 
emotion conveyed through intonation and pitch. This 
approach is inspired from the communication between 
mothers and their babies in the age where no words 
are yet understood. Still many emotional states can 
be communicated in exaggerated form using, what 
is commonly know as baby-language. Similar to the 
gibberish language Mubin, Bartneck & Feijs (2010) have 
also created a new language for the interaction between 
humans and robots as a form of code language. Their 
goal is to continue using speech recognition in robots 
as a form of input, but simplify it by creating a language 
specifically for this interaction, focusing on making it as 
recognizable for the robot as possible, while being easy 
for humans to learn.
Yet another approach is the use of non-linguistic 
utterances. The use of non-linguistic utterances is 
not as rich a language as natural speech, but does 
pose some clear advantages at the present state of 
technological development. It is easier to produce, can 
enrich the communication by containing emotional cues 
and is multi-lingual/cultural (Read & Belpaeme 2013). 
At the same time non-linguistic utterances will not raise 
expectations about a robots abilities to an unreasonably 
high level, setting the user up for disappointment 
(Schwenk & Arras 2014).
==PAGE:33==
1 8   |  
Many of the articles studied articles point to the same 
adjustable parameters to effectively differentiate 
emotional sounds for robots and make them easier 
to understand. These include sound pitch, timbre, 
frequency, duration, volume and rhythm (Ayesh 2006, 
Kobayashi & Wada 2005, Niculescu, van Dijk, Nijholt & 
See 2011, Read & Belpaeme 2012). Other researchers 
have seen positive results, when using modified human 
or animal noises in designing robot sounds (Haring, Bee 
& Andre 2011) or even analysing the sounds of movie 
robots R2-D2 and WALL-E (Jee, Jeong, Kim & Kobayashi 
2010).
2 . 2 . 3   A P P E A R A N C E
While it is generally believed that appearance does 
matter in robot design, the correct approach is still 
discussed. Although some studies have revealed that 
humanoids prove to be more accepted for social tasks 
than non-humanoids (Choi & Kwak 2015, Goetz, Kiesler 
& Powers 2003), others look at the returning projection 
of the Uncanny Valley (Woods, Dautenhahn & Schulz 
2004, Yamamoto, Tanaka, Kobayashi, Kozima & Hashiya 
2009). As mentioned earlier, anthropomorphic and 
zoomorphic elements in robots’ visual appearance 
can have a positive effect, but getting too close to the 
human look creates an uneasy feeling in many viewers, 
as well as increased expectations to the robots abilities.
A team that has taken the appearance into account 
is the previously mentioned Jibo robot team, 
presenting the world’s first social robot for the home. 
Jibo’s appearance is designed to be in between an 
anthropomorphic and abstract object to better fit in 
with other products for the home (Team Jibo 2015a). 
The human resemblance in the design is taken from 
the scientific concept Kindenschema presented by 
Konrad Lorenz in the 1940s. Kindenschema uses the 
natural urge in humans to care for a creature with 
the same proportions as a human child - big head 
compared to the body to ”... maximise cuteness or the 
attractiveness associated with little kids” (Team Jibo 
2015a).
When looking at robots with social behaviour, 
different studies of current robots and their perceived 
appropriate tasks, reveal that humanoids should be 
better suited for social tasks, while machine-like robots 
are seen as more appropriate for e.g. surveillance, 
police jobs and other investigative jobs (Goetz et al. 
2003, Lohse, Hegel, Swadzba, Rohlfing, Wachsmuth & 
Wrede 2001, Destephe, Brandao, Kishi, Zecca, Hashimoto 
& Takanishi 2015). In their study Choi & Kwak (2015) 
conclude that a humanoid, or human-oriented robot as 
they call it, was more effective than a product-oriented 
robot, in social context, while the opposite result was 
revealed in a task-oriented context. This is opposed 
by the result of a trial of robots working with kids with 
autism in a playful setting. Here the kids had a better 
response to interaction with a plain featureless robot 
than a human-like robot (Robins & Dautenhahn 2004). 
Furthermore a study of people’s reaction to robots in 
distress show that there was no significant changes in 
reaction towards a humanoid or non-humanoid robot 
(Briggs, Gessell, Dunlap & Scheutz 2014).
Counter to what one might believe, there are no 
indication in literature that the use of changing colors 
is an effective way to communicate emotions in 
robots (Haring et al. 2011). The interpretation of most 
colors is too ambiguous and too dependent on the 
recipients cultural background to communicate anything 
clearly which leads us back to the section on Cultural 
References (2.1.3 på side 15).
Only a small variety of robot appearances have been 
investigated and the general definition of when a 
L I T E R AT U R E  R E V I E W
==PAGE:34==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   1 9
robot is humanistic is very broad, which affects the 
results. For example the robot investigated by Choi & 
Kwak (2015) and Goetz et al. (2003) and the android 
description by Li, Florendo, Miller, Ishiguro & Saygin 
(2015) are very different in both human-likeness and 
detail, and within the same study the same humanoid 
robot was perceived as not quite human by some 
participants, while others found it very human-like 
(Destephe et al. 2015). Von der Pütten & Krämer 
(2012) have clustered the appearance of different 
robots into six categories based on an online survey 
of 151 participants about human-likeness, likeability, 
dominance and threat. The clusters were (see also 
figure 2–1):
1.	
Small robots, rated as likeable, not threatening, 
submissive, rather not human-like and medium 
mechanical
2.	
Rather small, not threatening, submissive, not 
human-like and medium mechanical, but less 
likeable and more unfamiliar
3.	
Essentially familiar, human-like and not 
mechanical, but threatening and less likeable
4.	
Essentially familiar, human-like and not 
mechanical, but highly likeable and less 
threatening
5.	
Likeable, threatening, dominant, rather 
unfamiliar, rather not human-like and very 
mechanical
6.	
Human-like but not rather mechanical robots 
rated as rather not likeable, threatening, 
submissive and very unfamiliar.
It is interesting to see that the first cluster, described 
as rather not human-like, contains Asimo and Nao, 
two of the robots often used as perfect examples of 
humanoids in research (Vincent et al. 2015, Höflich & El 
Bayed 2015, Peng, Zhou, Hu, Chao & Li 2015). Interesting 
as well is the seemingly experienced Uncanny Valley in 
cluster 3, but not in cluster 4. Here they are categorized 
as threatening and less likeable and highly likeable and 
less threatening respectively, even though they both 
are an attempt to get as close to a human duplicate 
as possible. It seems there is a gap in the robotic 
appearance investigation between droid-like and 
function-oriented robots that do not have animalistic or 
human-like appearance. Finding a middle ground seems 
to hold a lot of promise.
2 . 3   C U R R E N T 
R O B O T I C  D E S I G N 
T O O L S ,  M E T H O D S  A N D 
F R A M E W O R K S
Already there has been some work done on creating 
robotic design tools, methods and frameworks. 
Bartneck & Forlizzi (2004) have presented a framework 
for classifying social robots. They classify them in 5 
categories: Form (from abstract to anthropomorphic), 
Modality (from unimodal to multimodal), Social Norms 
(from no knowledge of social norms to full knowledge 
of social norms), Autonomy (from no autonomy to 
fully autonomous) and Interactivity (from no casual 
behaviour to fully casual behaviour). The framework 
can be used to position social robots in relation to each 
other and in defining the desired characteristics of new 
robots. Similarly Goodrich & Schultz (2007) identifies 5 
attributes that affect the interaction between humans 
and robots: The level and behaviour of autonomy, the 
Nature of information exchange, the Structure of the 
team, the Adaptation, learning and training of people 
and the robot and the Shape of the task. There are 
some similarities between the two, but where the first is 
focused on the robot as entity the second also takes the 
context of the robot into consideration.
==PAGE:35==
2 0   |  
Going in that direction Mutlu (2006) has created a 
conceptual framework for understanding the social 
aspects of interaction with robots. He divides the 
interaction in User attributes, Product attributes and 
Social context of use as the aspects that affect the 
social interaction. 
These frameworks and attributes have largely been 
created from the experience of the researchers, they 
have not been tested on a larger scale. Even then they 
agree to a great extend on which are the important 
aspects of HRI and with other research in the area. They 
are however more focused on describing robots than 
developing them.
Some researchers are not focused on developing a 
design process for HRI, but do present and reflect 
on their own design process. This is true for Szafir 
et al. (2015) as they explored the design space of 
communicating a flying robots intentions. They 
figure 2–1.	THE 6 CLUSTERS OF ROBOTIC APPEARANCE SURVEY BY VON DER PÜTTEN & KRÄMER (2012).
L I T E R AT U R E  R E V I E W
==PAGE:36==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   2 1
describe their process as an iterative one, where 
they started out analysing the design constraints and 
specifications, followed by an iterative ideation and 
prototype building. They used reference designs based 
on metaphors of common user experiences and found 
it useful in creating the right mental models within test 
participants. Also Lee et al. (2009) present their design 
process in their work with the Snackbot. Similar to Szafir 
et al.’s their process start out with a need analysis 
exploring the context of the robot, followed by form 
giving and interaction design and finally documentation 
and evaluation. They reflect that it is important to have a 
holistic view of the robot while developing, to be able to 
integrate different features properly.
The tools to work holistically with the design of HRI are 
largely missing in the literature. Most research focus 
on one modality at the time. Sauppé & Mutlu (2014) 
have for instance developed an interesting library of 
interaction patterns, but only for verbal interaction. 
Others like Breemen (2004b) and Takayama, Dooley 
& Ju (2011) have focused on using the principles of 
animation in the design of robots’ movements.
Several researchers have worked on integrating 
designers more in the development of HRI, but the 
tools they have come up with are mostly graphical 
programming languages, where designers can compose 
interaction sequences from pre-defined blocks of 
behaviour (Huang & Mutlu 2012, Sauppé & Mutlu 2014, 
Glas, Satake, Kanda & Hagita 2011, Breemen 2004b, 
Katragadda 1997, Lee, Kim & Kim 2007). These tools 
all get the feedback that they are easy to use, but very 
restrictive. It is not possible for the designers to go back 
a step and develop new behaviours or create holistic 
concepts for the robot’s interaction.
Finally there has been some work on creating 
frameworks for evaluating HRI design. Young et al. (2011) 
present three perspectives through witch a design can 
be evaluated:
1.	
Visceral factors of interaction (largely instinctual 
reactions to interaction with robots)
2.	
Social mechanics (social signals and responses 
used in communication)
3.	
Social structures (the context of the interaction, 
that both influences and is influenced by the 
robot)
They propose that the evaluation of HRI design can 
benefit from these perspectives by offering a vocabulary 
to articulate the social characteristics of the interaction, 
by keeping focus on social issues of importance, and 
by being used to dissect and direct data exploration in 
studying the design. They also propose that the same 
perspectives can be used in a brainstorming session 
with a method they call an Interaction Experience Map. 
Here they explore the user experience iteratively from 
both a human-centered and a robot-centered view, 
brainstorming on interaction scenarios, experience 
possibilities and alternate interaction scenarios in the 
human-centered view and on design characteristics, 
reactions and alternate reactions in the robot-centered 
view. As the authors describe this is a heuristic method, 
where the results are based purely on the designers 
own sense of judgement. This method is the closest 
we have come to a tool for HRI design, but it is still 
lacking tangibility and is best used in explorative and 
experimental stages of development.

3 . 1   R E S E A R C H 
C L A R I F I C A T I O N
The research phase consisted of an exploratory 
literature study, where the overall subject of HRI was 
researched, followed by a more specific look into the 
three dimensions; movement, sound and appearance, 
and the existing HRI methods, tools and frameworks in 
literature. This was done to gain a better understanding 
of the field and to find evidence that could support our 
assumption that HRI design methods were generally 
missing in literature. The method of the literature 
study was to gather articles with the newest research 
in robotics and design methods from online search 
engines like DTU FindIt, Google Scholar and relevant 
books.
3 . 2   D E S C R I P T I V E 
S T U D I E S
This phase consisted of 13 qualitative interviews that 
were conducted to research the current process of 
robotic development and HRI design in Denmark 
as well as current expert practice within the areas: 
Movement, sound and appearance. Six interviews 
were done with representatives of the design teams 
from four different companies: Teknologisk Institut, 
Blue Ocean Robotics, MiR and LT Automation. These 
were done to understand the thought process of the 
practitioners and identify and specify their need for 
new methods. In addition the expert practice in the 
three interaction dimensions were explored outside of 
robotics in the areas of animation, choreography, film, 
industrial-, interaction- and sound design to provide 
a novel and reflected perspective on the subject and 
inspiration for new method development.
The interviews were all between 30 min. and 1 hour 
situated at the interviewees location of work. A 
semi-structured approach was used with an open-
questioned personalised interview guide for each 
subject, reserving the right to ask supplementary 
questions and cut questions found irrelevant on the 
scene. Each interview began with an explanation of the 
purpose of the interview and ended with the offer to 
supply the finished report. A high degree of descriptive 
questions were used to let the informants walk us 
through their process in their own words, as adviced 
I N  T H E  F O L LO W I N G  W E  P R E S E N T  T H E  M E T H O D O LO GY  BY  W H I C H  T H I S

METHODOLOGY
==PAGE:39==
24   |  M E T H O D O LO GY
by Spradley (1979). In each interview an interviewer and 
a note taker was pointed out within the project group 
beforehand and the conversations were furthermore 
recorded on a dictaphone.
The interviews were analysed with a version of a KJ-
Chart (Hanington & Martin 2012), clustering statements 
into categories, to establish relations between the data 
and locate the main needs. 
3 . 3   P R E S C R I P T I V E 
S T U D I E S
The first draft of the new methods were developed 
during a partly creative and partly structured design 
process. Brainstorm sessions based on the interview 
notes and the found needs created the groundwork for 
the first ideas and concepts. The best concepts were 
singled out and further developed to work as a base for 
the first workshop.
The concepts were tested in three workshops; two with 
design students putting the methods to use and one 
with professionals discussing them in plenum. The first 
workshop was held with nine engineering students at 
their university and helped to create an initial evaluation 
of the methods at an early concept state. Through this 
workshops we sought a better understanding of how 
the methods would be received and used, which ones 
worked in their current form and which needed to be 
revised or discarded. The concepts were then adjusted 
based on feedback and observations and re-tested in a 
second workshop with four design engineer students. 
Again the design of the methods were refined based on 
feedback and observations. 
The third workshop was held with five professional 
robot developers from three danish companies to get 
feedback from a professional user group and come one 
step closer to the intended use of the methods. The 
feedback obtained from the final workshop provided 
knowledge of the methods’ usefulness and possible 
implementation, as well as helped form the final toolbox.

4 . 1   C U R R E N T  R O B O T 
D E V E L O P M E N T 
P R A C T I C E
The first round of interviews aimed to create an 
overview of the current development processes and 
tools used in Danish companies taking on the new 
wave of robotic development. This was done by several 
exploratory interviews with robotic developers in small, 
medium and larger Danish companies.
In this qualitative study we have explored the current 
ways of dealing with robot development in Denmark, 
the general process, the steps they go through and the 
methods used. The interviews were carried out mainly 
as face to face conversations (5 subjects) or in one 
case as a video call (1 subject). The informants chosen 
for the interviews were all working with development of 
robots in the following Danish companies:
•	
BLUE OCEAN ROBOTICS distributes robots 
within Denmark and develops specialised 
robot solutions in collaborations with their 
customers. It is a medium company of around 
50 employees in Denmark founded in 2012.
•	
LT AUTOMATION is an automation company that 
works with developing welfare technological 
solutions within the health and hospital sector. 
It is a small company of 6 employees founded 
in 2009.
•	
MIR (Mobile Industrial Robots) develops an 
autonomous mobile robotic platform for 
internal transportation. It is a small start-up 
company of 12 employees founded in 2013.
•	
Last but not least, TEKNOLOGISK INSTITUT, 
a consulting firm that by developing novel 
technology solutions for companies creates 
a bridge between the universities knowledge 
resources and the companies in Denmark. It is 
a bigger company of 1000+ employees, with 34 
people just in the robotic development sector.
These companies have been chosen to participate, 
mainly based on their need for dealing with the human
