DESCRIPTIVE STUDIES
==PAGE:43==
2 8   |  D ES C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
perspective in robotic development and on their broad 
field of customers and projects. Examples of their 
products can be seen in figure 4–1, figure 4–2 and 
figure 4–3.
4 . 1 . 1   F I N D I N G S
T H E  D E V E L O P M E N T  P R O C E S S
The process of developing robotic solutions were 
very different in each of the interviewed companies 
and was often based on previous experience. At Blue 
Ocean Robotics they had a very clear process plan 
with fixed steps that they always went through. They 
used a Stage Gate Model 1 and SCRUM 2 to structure 
their work. At Teknologisk institut they had three tracks 
of development defined; Requirement specification, 
Technology development, and Test cases, but had a 
very chaotic process, in their own words, where new 
assignments kept popping up. They tried to divide the 
projects into smaller assignments and put in milestones 
to keep on track. As a start-up company MiR had a more 
iterative process, where problems were solved one 
at the time in order of urgency, with less of an overall 
structure.
The companies did however have some commonalities 
in their processes. They all seemed to have three stages 
in their projects or iterations. They began with what we 
will call an analysis phase, followed by a development 
phase and ending in a test phase. These can be seen 
illustrated in figure 4–4.
1  The Stage Gate Model is a project management technique that divides 
a development process in different stages separated by gates. Each 
gate marks the end of a phase and results in a meeting where it is 
decided whether to continue the project or not based on the available 
information (Stage-Gate International n.d.).
2  SCRUM is an agile software development methodology for managing 
product development. It divides the development in smaller scrum 
periods, where the whole team is working to reach a common goal. It 
revolves around the key principle that a development process cannot be 
planned from the start and therefore strives to make it flexible enough 
to accommodate for unforeseen issues or changing goals (Scrum n.d.).
figure 4–1.	UV-STERILIZER ROBOT BY BLUE OCEAN ROBOTICS 
(UV-STERILIZER ROBOT N.D.) 
figure 4–2.	ROBERT, A PHYSIOTHERAPY ROBOT BY LT AUTOMATION 
(GENOPTRÆNINGSROBOTTEN ROBERT® N.D.) 
figure 4–3.	MIR100, A MOBILE TRANSPORTATION ROBOT BY MIR 
(MIR100 N.D.) 
==PAGE:44==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   2 9
figure 4–4.	OVERVIEW OF THE COMMON DESIGN PROCESS AND METHODS USED IN CURRENT ROBOTIC DEVELOPMENT.
A n a l y s i s 
By analysis we here mean the process of determining 
the need and requirements of the robotic product. What 
comes before the actual development. Their analysis 
phase was often more extensive in the beginning of 
a project, but research phases could also be carried 
out in the beginning of a new iteration. One part of the 
analysis that all of them mentioned was researching 
user and/or customer needs as one of the first steps in 
their process.
”It works well to visit the customer and get 
acquainted with their needs and problems. It is 
important to me, that we do that, preferably several 
times. It is easy to become fascinated with the 
technology and wanting to integrate it without it 
having a point.” 
- MALENE TOFVESON NIBE, DESIGN ENGINEER AT TEKNOLOGISK 
INSTITUT (SEE APPENDIX A.2.4)
All the companies had this realisation, that to develop 
the right products they needed to know the problems 
they should solve and the challenges they could face in 
implementation.
”When robots share a working space with humans, 
then the technology is only 20% of the solution. The 
rest is the system and cooperation. The technology 
needs to fit to the organisation” 
- LARS DALGAARD, TEAM LEADER AT TEKNOLOGISK INSTITUT 
(SEE APPENDIX A.2.2)
Another common part of the analysis phase was 
looking at the business model of the product and what 
alternatives existed in the marked. Both Lasse Thomsen 
from LT Automation and Thomas Rubæk from Blue 
Ocean Robotics mentioned how they usually research 
the marked in the early stages of a project, looking 
for alternatives and estimating if there was a viable 
business case for the product they wanted to develop.
D e v e l o p m e n t 
The development phase was often very iterative in 
the interviewed companies. This was promoted as an 
effective process, where ideas could be tested quickly 
and have several improvement loops before going out 
to the customers. The development phase was mostly 
focused on solving technical issues and less so on 
the design of the robot or interface. The development 
process still varied a bit in each of the companies 
==PAGE:45==
3 0   |  D ES C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
though. As a small start-up company MiR did not have 
the resources to hire a dedicated designer, why most 
of the design was created in the beginning through 
student projects and afterwards revisited by software 
engineers and the CTO. At LT automation, they chose 
to outsource some of the design work on a project 
basis, keeping only core competences inside the 
company. The projects done by Teknologisk Institut 
seldom reached a commercialization level, so they 
could manage only needing the design on a general 
level. Blue Ocean Robotics, working as a consulting firm, 
where the only company interviewed with a generic 
design process that ended up with another team taking 
over the launch and finish.
T e s t 
All the interviewed companies ended up with a test 
phase at the end of their development process. 
Commonly tests were done in three stages. First 
prototypes were tested internally in the companies, later 
with a few users in context and in the end the products 
were distributed to all users. The tests were used to 
get feedback on the products and early criticism, to be 
able to improve the solutions before going to broader 
marked.
M E T H O D S  A N D  T O O L S  U S E D  I N 
R O B O T I C  D E V E L O P M E N T
All the companies mentioned different methods and 
tools used during their development process. In general 
they had a pretty good idea of how to define the project 
and had several methods to scope out the problem 
to be solved, define the requirements for the robots 
and determine the appropriate technology for the job. 
On the other hand it was a little more vague, what 
methods and tools were used for the design process. 
Many of the companies mentioned idea generation and 
brainstorming as methods used at this stage, but had 
difficulties defining it more specifically.
”We are working to concretise our models. It is hard 
to create general methods that work in all projects. 
Specifically in the design phase we are missing some 
concrete tools, which we are trying to develop. It 
helps us to have these tools. A project that is not 
managed costs more hours. If we don’t have tools we 
will exceed our budgets.” 
- THOMAS RUBÆK, PROJECT MANAGER AT BLUE OCEAN 
ROBOTICS (SEE APPENDIX A.2.6)
A few methods were mentioned though specifically 
for the design phase. At Teknologisk Institut Malene 
Tofveson Nibe had good experiences using Negative 
Brainstorming3 in workshops with clients. ”People think 
it is a lot easier to come up with the bad ideas”, as 
well as Role Perspectives and Personas; ”It gets you 
thinking out of the box, when you take on a specific 
perspective” (see appendix A.2.4). At MiR they used lo-fi 
prototypes extensively in the early stages of the design 
process. Using cardboard boxes they had experimented 
with the size of the robot, to make it useful for its task, 
but not threatening to the users. All before there was 
any automation involved.
Both Teknologisk Institut and LT Automation were aware 
of the intimidating effect robots could have on the users. 
But where Teknologisk Institut solved this problem by 
giving more information on the robot to the users, LT 
Automation had a different approach. They had had a 
mascot logo designed for the robot, before the actual 
3  A negative brainstorm is one in which it is not the initial focus to 
come up with good solutions to a problem. Instead the participants 
try to come up with the worst imaginable solutions. This is done 
to achieve a creative atmosphere and analyse the problem from a 
different perspective. Often in negative brainstorms the bad solutions 
are attempted to be changed into working solutions in the end (Higgins 
1994).
==PAGE:46==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   3 1
robot. The mascot was suppose to be a personification 
of the robot, making it more approachable.
”The robot could not seem deterrent, so we had a 
designer in Indonesia make a small logo figure to 
demystify the robot. Then the users think of a cute 
little figure named Robert and are not scared any 
more.” 
- LASSE THOMSEN, CEO AT LT AUTOMATION (SEE APPENDIX 
A.2.3).
In the design phase the company had used the mascot 
as inspiration for the form giving of the actual robot to 
strengthen the connection between the two (see figure 
4–5).
LT Automation had also started using 3D animation as 
part of their design process. They build a CAD model 
of the robot and animated different use-scenarios to 
be able to show it to clients and users. In their view it 
create a better understanding of the robot and was a 
way for them to get feedback before building it.
”An animation costs 25.000 DKK, a prototype costs 
1 million DKK, so it pays off to make an animation, if 
something needs to be changed later on.”
- LASSE THOMSEN, CEO AT LT AUTOMATION (SEE APPENDIX 
A.2.3).
4 . 1 . 2   M A I N  T A K E A WAY S
From the interviews we saw that most of the companies 
had the overall development process under control. 
They were very capable of structuring their work in 
a way that suited their needs. They already knew to 
involve the users, research the marked and conduct 
tests in context to improve their product. They also had 
the skills necessary to come up with novel technological 
solutions. The design phase however, and especially 
the development of interaction design was a little more 
fuzzy. The steps involved were created from project to 
project, depending on context and the people involved. 
They did not have a fixed toolbox ready for use.
Most of the methods mentioned by the interviewees 
were not specific to development of robotic products. 
We saw a gab in the design phase of the development 
process of these products (see appendix B). Specifically 
we saw that the companies needed new tools for:
figure 4–5.	LOGO FIGURE FOR THE ROBOT ROBERT BY LT AUTOMATION (ROBERT FIGURE N.D.).
==PAGE:47==
32   |  D ES C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
M a n a g i n g  u s e r  e x p e c t a t i o n s  i n 
t h e  i n t e r a c t i o n  w i t h  r o b o t s
The companies saw how the design of a robot had a big 
influence on if the expectations of the users were met 
and if the robotic product was used or misused. They 
did however not have any tools available to account for 
this in the development.
I n c o r p o r a t i n g  a n t h r o p o m o r p h i c 
e l e m e n t s  i n  d e s i g n s
As seen from the literature study the use of 
anthropomorphic or zoomorphic features can have a 
positive effect on HRI. This was not on the agenda in the 
robotic companies however.
F i t t i n g  r o b o t s  t o  t h e i r  c o n t e x t 
All the companies talked about the importance of 
making robotic solutions fit to their context, but did 
not know how to do that for all aspects of the robot. 
Sound and motion was often neglected as ways to make 
the users feel more familiar with and secure around a 
product.
C r e a t i n g  h o l i s t i c  d e s i g n 
c o n c e p t s ,  g u i d i n g  a l l  d i m e n s i o n s 
o f  d e s i g n 
The interviewed companies did not have holistic 
concepts for the design of the developed robots, making 
the overall expression scattered and unclear to users.
U n d e r s t a n d i n g  t h e  p e r s p e c t i v e 
o f  u s e r s  i n  t h e i r  m e e t i n g  w i t h  a 
r o b o t 
During the analysis phase all companies did user 
studies, but still had problems truly understanding the 
mental model of users, when faced with a robot as a 
new tool.
T e s t i n g  t h e  i n t e r a c t i o n  w i t h 
a  r o b o t  b e f o r e  r e a c h i n g  a  f u l l 
p r o t o t y p e 
In most of the companies the interaction with a robot 
was only tested when they had a working prototype, 
at a stage where it can be very expensive to redesign 
hardware components. Testing the interaction earlier in 
the process would allow them to make less expensive 
changes.
4 . 2   A N A L O G O U S 
D E V E L O P M E N T  O F 
M O V E M E N T ,  S O U N D  A N D 
V I S U A L  A P P E A R A N C E
The second round of interviews aimed to explore the 
range of creative processes within the three dimensions; 
sound, motion and appearance, outside of the 
development of HRI. Through interviews with experts 
in each of the fields we sought a better understanding 
of the methods used in the creation process and how 
their approach to a design challenge would play out. 
This provided an interesting base of inspiration for the 
development of HRI design methods .
The interviews were carried out as face to face 
conversations (8 subjects). The participants chosen for 
this study was deliberately spread out between experts 
in motion, sound and visual appearance, with different 
backgrounds and titles. The broad spectre was selected 
to see similarities and differences between the creative 
development processes and give us a broad variety of 
inspiration sources. The participants were:
•	
ANNE KATRINE FRIIS, a freelance dance 
choreographer.
==PAGE:48==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   3 3
•	
JAKOB BRAHE-PEDERSEN, an experienced 
industrial designer leading his own consultancy 
company Brahe-Design.
•	
JAKOB WELNER, a freelance 3D animator 
working with commercial and movie 
productions.
•	
JESPER ANKARFELDT, a freelance media 
composer working on projects designing the 
soundscape of apps, games and movies.
•	
KARSTEN DINES, a sound designer working for 
the company Freezone with sounddesign for 
radio, tv and movie productions.
•	
MARTINA PAGURA, an interaction designer 
working as a consultant at CIID Concultancy.
•	
SEBASTIAN CORDES, a documentary film director 
and teacher within film technique at Station 
Next.
4 . 2 . 1   F I N D I N G S
T H E  C R E A T I V E  P R O C E S S
The creative process of each of the subjects had a lot of 
commonalities even though they were experts in very 
different fields. The common process is illustrated in 
figure 4–6.
I d e a / T a s k  U n d e r s t a n d i n g 
All of the subjects went through an idea/task 
understanding phase, where they either got an idea 
themselves or were hired to do a specific task. In both 
cases they needed understanding of what the project 
was going to demand of them and how to plan for it. 
This phase ended up in a more or less clear project 
description, either provided by the task giver or by 
themselves. 
R e s e a r c h 
Secondly a research and framing phase helped to clarify 
what the project entailed and frame the future work. The 
subjects searched for inspiration from existing work, the 
internet, in videos and in nature. In this phase decisions 
about where the project was going and what creative 
style should be aimed for were made. 
C r e a t i v e  D e v e l o p m e n t 
Next followed a creative process, where a rough first 
draft was developed and the project took form. Here the 
participants varied, although they all seemed to build 
figure 4–6.	OVERVIEW OF THE GENERAL CREATIVE PROCESS OBTAINED BY INTERVIEWS IN STUDY 2
==PAGE:49==
3 4   |  D ES C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
ideas and concepts upon a frame of reference within 
their field and the subject of their ongoing project.
P r o t o t y p e  &  T e s t 
Depending on the expectations for a final presentation 
and product, the draft was iterated on an undefined 
amount of times, often with feedback loops from the 
customers, to make it ready for implementation and 
hand-in. The subjects had a hard time identifying 
methods they used in this process. Often they used 
their intuition, experience and did trial and error until 
they landed on a satisfying result.
M E T H O D S  A N D  T O O L S
The experts each had their own set of tools and 
methods and a workspace intended for them. Jesper 
Ankarfeldt and Karsten Dines both had access to a 
studio, where they could tape and mix sounds. Their 
main tools were complex music programs as well as 
instruments and other objects that could be used to 
create sound effects. At Kartens studio they had a whole 
bookcase full of random objects that had good sounds, 
as well as different types of floors hidden under a hatch 
to be able to make the right walking sounds for different 
environments.
Jakob Welner was also used to work by a computer, 
modelling animations and test them out as he went. 
He mentioned that a common method in animation is 
a method called a Character Bible. He described it as a 
set of directories for a character, including for instance 
it’s background, motivations and quirks, to be used by a 
team of animators to agree on how the character would 
act in certain situations.
”You try to establish, who is this person really? So of 
course some of it is written in the manuscript, but it 
creates a lot more depth the more you can come up 
with about this person and what they experienced as 
a child and how their upbringing was. It is interesting 
to see how much you can gain from this. Even things 
you don’t necessarily think, is being expressed in the 
acting. But if you have it in the back of your head 
when making a choice, it becomes clear that you 
are making this choice based on something in the 
characters background that leads up to this moment. 
It really is putting the character in context to its story 
which provides a lot of data.” 
- JAKOB WELNER, ANIMATOR, (SEE APPENDIX A.3.3)
In addition he had previously used video taping to study 
the movements of squirrels on a squirrel farm and 
interviewed a football coach on football tricks to get the 
context right for a particular commercial.
Similarly, Choreographer Anne Katrine Friis mentioned 
that she studied context by watching YouTube videos 
to get inspiration from specific environments for 
her choreographies, be it hiphop or stewardesses. 
Otherwise she worked a lot with her own body, testing 
moves out in front of a mirror.
For Jakob Brahe his most important tools were pen and 
paper. When sketching he was able to visualize his ideas 
and communicate them to clients. While early sketches 
were made by hand, more detailed ones were made 
with computer programs, such as CAD software.
Film Director Sebastian Cordes used a method he had 
developed himself when shooting film. He established 
his own abstract guidelines every time he started a 
new project to make sure it would be going towards 
something novel and undiscovered.
One method they all described were how they build 
upon references from their daily life, the projects context 
==PAGE:50==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   3 5
and/or in the given field of knowledge, which created a 
base for brainstorming on an idea or concept. 
4 . 2 . 2   M A I N  T A K E A WAY S
From the interviews we saw that a lot of the experts’ 
processes were very similar in their structure and that 
their creative methods to a large degree came out of 
experience. Some of the interesting takeaways from 
the interviews were especially the methods mentioned 
that could easily inspire new HRI methods. With respect 
to sound this was e.g. the idea of having a “library” 
of instruments and objects to test out different sound 
effects. From animation we were inspired by the idea 
of a Character Bible to create more depth in robots, but 
also from the idea of using video both as inspiration 
and as a mean of testing movements, while sketching 
is an obvious method when it comes to working with 
appearance. 
Last but not least we noticed how all of the experts put 
an immense importance on understanding the context 
of their work before developing anything. 
”When you design interactions the context is very 
important. ... What is easy to use for an elderly person 
that lives in an elderly home is very different than 
what an astronaut might want out of a dashboard.” 
- MARTINA PAGURA, INTERACTION DESIGNER AT CIID (SEE 
APPENDIX A.3.6)
”There is no manual (red. for good sound design), 
even though a lot of people want to make one. It is all 
about the context and what follows visually. A yelling 
voice can be both negative and positive in another 
context.” 
- JESPER ANKARFELDT, FREELANCE MEDIA COMPOSER (SEE 
APPENDIX A.3.4).
This was also an important takeaway that we made note 
of in the further work.

5 . 1   D E V E L O P M E N T  O F 
T H E  M E T H O D S
The methods went through a creative development 
process from problem definition, through ideation and 
conceptualization ending in eight concepts. From there 
four were chosen to be tested in a workshop with 
students. After the first workshop the methods were 
iterated on and re-evaluated in a second and third 
workshop with students and professionals respectively. 
It was a process alternating between divergent 
and convergent phases. The process can be seen 
visualized in figure 5–1. The following will be structured 
according to these phases and go through all three 
iterations for each of the methods in succession. 
5 . 1 . 1   P R O B L E M  D E F I N I T I O N
From the interviews with professionals in the robotic 
industry, issues were found unaccounted for in the 
current robotic development process and reformulated 
into a problem definition to insure the right focus of the 
ideation:
•	
How to account for user expectations in the 
interaction with robotic products
•	
How to incorporate anthropomorphic and 
zoomorphic elements in robotic designs
•	
How to fit a robotic solution to it’s context
•	
How to create a holistic HRI design concept
•	
How to create understanding for the users 
perspective
•	
How to test interactions quickly and at low cost
5 . 1 . 2   I D E A T I O N  A N D 
C O N C E P T U A L I Z A T I O N
The ideation was done in four sessions writing down 
ideas on post-it notes for later comparison and 
synthesis. First an open brainstorm was conducted, 
relieving us of all the ideas gathered during the 
literature review and interviews. The three next 
sessions each focused on one of the three dimensions 
sound, appearance and motion to come up with ideas 
for tools or methods dedicated to the development of

==PAGE:54==

design within that dimension (see figure 5–2). Between 
session the ideas were presented and discussed in the 
group to inspire new ideas. To ensure that the solution 
space had been thoroughly explored the ideas where 
categorised according to the defined problems and 
an additional brainstorm was conducted to account 
for missing solutions to any of the problem areas (see 
figure 5–3, appendix C and D).
After collecting a great variety of ideas these where 
synthesised during conceptualization. Several of them 
could be combined to create more holistic concepts 
for methods. During this process eight concepts were 
created (see appendix E).
All eight concepts were then detailed further and 
later discussed and evaluated based on their novelty, 
efficiency and ability to fit in with designers in the 
robotic development environment. The evaluation was 
done based on the following criteria; time efficiency, 
prerequisites already on team, cost and investment, 
novelty, integration in environment, learnability, 
effectiveness, flexibility and project feasibility (see 
appendix E). Four concepts were chosen for testing in a 
workshop with students. They were Robot Personality, 
the Zombie, Context of the Robot and Scenario. 
5 . 1 . 3   W O R K S H O P  S E T U P S
The set-ups of all three workshops have been illustrated 
in table 5–1. Additional information and pictures from 
the workshops can be found in appendix F.
5 . 1 . 4   M E T H O D  1  -  R O B O T 
P E R S O N A L I T Y
Working with the first two problem statements; user 
expectations in the interaction with robotic products 
and incorporation of anthropomorphic elements in the 
design, the purpose of the Robot Personality method 
was to create robotic products with a holistic character 
that could develop over time. According to Kanda et 
al. (2004) and Simmons et al. (2011) this could create 
a more dynamic relationship between the robot and 
user and support a continuous interest in interaction by

table 5–1.	 SETUP OF THE EXPLORATORY WORKSHOPS
==PAGE:56==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   41
the user. The concept was to let a design team fill out 
a template with the desired characteristics of the robot 
they were about to develop, as a basis for further design 
decisions. The template asked for non-tangible traits of 
the robot, such as qualities, favourite quote, motivations 
and frustrations (See the development of the method 
in figure 5–4). The output was a document that could 
be revisited at any stage of the design process to guide 
design decisions or as a communication tool to share 
the vision for the robotic concept. See appendix figure 
H–10.
The method could help the design team to see the 
developed robot as an autonomous actor instead of a 
passive product. This was important because the users 
of robots already viewed them this way (Young et al. 
2011) and it is crucial for a design team to understand 
and design for the mental model of the users (Norman 
1983). Discussing the vision for the personality of a 
robot would create an overall plan with the design. 
Instead of making decisions on a problem basis, the 
method would provide an overall guideline for the 
design, corresponding to a Design Specification (Cross 
2008) for the robot’s personality. The method was 
inspired from a method used in animation called a 
Character Bible (see section 4.2.1), where the back 
story and personality of an animation character is 
developed as a basis for an animation. In addition Robot 
Personality was similar to the method of developing 
Personas1, only here the personality of the robotic 
product and not the user was described.
I T E R A T I O N  1
Iteration 1 of Robot Personality was the result of a test 
during a workshop with 5 students, where the method 
1  Personas is a method to caputer the essens of an archetypical 
representation of intended users. It can be used to communicate and 
personlify behaviours, values and needs (van Boeijen, Daalhuizen, van 
der Schoor & Zijlstra 2014).
was used on an imaginary case. The method was very 
well liked in the first workshop, but three problems were 
observed.
C h o i c e  o f  p e r s o n a l i t y 
The foremost critical observation was that there was 
a lack of background for how the personality should 
be chosen. In the debriefing it was discussed that a 
natural starting point might be in the company profile. 
To bridge the gap to the company case when using the 
method, a pre-exercise was created based in the theory 
on categorizing of brand personality (Aaker 1997), to 
touch upon the brand profile and put the designers in 
the right mindset. This could also aid the design team in 
developing the personality of a robot, by priming them 
to base it in the company vision and not as a random 
choice. In the exercise four personality traits within five 
dimensions were rated from most to least descriptive 
of the brand in question. The five brand personality 
dimensions were Sincerity, Excitement, Competence, 
Sophistication and Ruggedness. We saw this as a 
great pre-exercise to the Robot Personality method, 
to accommodate for the lack of background in the 
personality chosen for the robot (see figure 5–5).
I n s u f f i c i e n t  c h a r a c t e r 
d e s c r i p t i o n 
In the steps of ideation after the method was used it 
was seen that the character description was insufficient 
for substantiated decision making. The method needed 
more background for entering relevant data to make it 
function better as a discussion tool. As a first iteration 
we wanted to see if a background in personality theory 
and assessment could provide a better basis for 
creating a holistic character. 
==PAGE:57==
4 2   |  
figure 5–4.	THE ITERATIONS OF ROBOTIC PERSONALITY
P R E S C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
==PAGE:58==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   4 3
A lot of test and assessment tools provide a schematic 
way of sorting the complexity of the human personality. 
By taking as our starting point that the personality of 
a human can be somewhat approximated into types 
or qualities, we tried to look at the theory behind two 
of the most recognized assessment tools to create 
a spread of personality traits that could provide the 
developer with figurative characteristics for the robotic 
product; The MBTI (The Myers & Briggs Foundation 
2015) and DiSC (Geier & Downey 1989). Based on the 
theory we decided to change the entire persona card to 
contain the best of the traits from the assessment tools, 
keeping the overall objective the same. 
The first workshop showed the space for describing 
the character to be too small in order to facilitate a 
descend discussion. We changed the task of describing 
the character in three qualities to a broader brainstorm 
on ideal characteristics, to get the minds of the design 
team flowing and to describe a more wholesome 
personality.
As the theory behind DiSC is focused on how a person 
is motivated and reflects on their vision, the questions 
of motivation and values behind the robot should 
provide an overall vision for how the robots attitude 
relates to its actions. By giving a robot intentions 
and motivation it builds up trust in the humanity of 
the robot’s behaviour, which underlines the ”Like-
Me” attitude mentioned earlier by Fong et al. (2003). 
Additionally, we wanted to let the team discuss the 
robot’s approach to it’s surroundings, which is also a 
part both MBTI and DiSC.
The theory on growth in personality by Allport (1961) 
and experiments with long term HRI by Kanda et al. 
(2004) encourages a progression in the personality 
of a robots over time, and we wanted to support this 
by providing grounds for letting the team create an 
evolving personality. The questions of what you learn 
about the robot over time and reversed should work as 
a discussion piece for the design team to think about, 
what would make the robot interesting for the user also 
in future interactions.
A  n e e d  o f  f a c i l i t a t i o n 
The participants mentioned a need for more structured 
guidance in the steps of the method. To accommodate 
for this we constructed the card in a step-by-step 
process initiated by the free brainstorm mentioned 
above, supported by follow-up questions to get around 
a greater persona, and scales from MBTI and DiSC to 
let a design team look at how a robot should react to 
its surroundings. Should it e.g. be introvert or extrovert, 
empathetic or not and so on. The steps were meant 
to help create a dynamic between structured and 
unstructured creative development during the use of 
the method. The result of the first iteration of the Robot 
Personality can be seen in appendix figure H–10.
I T E R A T I O N  2
Iteration 2 of Robot Personality was the result of a test 
during a workshop on an imaginary case by 4 students. 
This time as the first in a toolbox of four methods in 
succession. The problem areas from iteration 2 were 
tested which resulted in small changes.
C h o i c e  o f  p e r s o n a l i t y  a n d  l i n k  t o 
b r a n d 
During the second workshop the pre-exercise where the 
brand personality had to be described worked well, but 
took the team a longer time to complete than expected. 
They had issues understanding the true meaning of 
all of the words and the difference between them. In 
some categories they found that non of the words 
==PAGE:59==
4 4   |  
fit the brand and in others that all of them were very 
descriptive, why they had a hard time ranking them. 
For the second iteration of the methods, we therefore 
decided to change the exercise slightly. Instead of 
having to rank the words within each dimension, we 
changed the task to choosing the 5 most descriptive 
words from all the dimension, with the option to add 
any word the design team thought might be missing. 
This could just as well facilitate a discussion on brand 
personality without the frustration of ranking a list of 
non-descriptive words.
For the main exercise on describing the character 
of the robot, the expansion of the template was a 
clear improvement. The group quickly went into a 
deep discussion from the presented tasks. With the 
first task of brainstorming on characteristics for the 
robot, they were still a little prone to getting into 
the functions of the robot though and had difficulty 
sticking to a discussion of the abstract personality of 
the robot. To adjust for this we changed the wording 
of the template heading from ”Robotic Character” 
to ”Robot Personality” and the task heading from 
”Character Brainstorm” to ”Personality Characteristics” 
to emphasis and encourage the mindset that this was 
about describing a personality for the robot and not the 
characteristics of a product.
B r o a d e n e d  s e n s e  o f  c h a r a c t e r 
The new follow-up questions that were formulated in 
the first iteration of the method worked very well in 
the second workshop to get the team to think about 
the robot as an autonomous actor with intentions and 
figure 5–5.	THE ITERATIONS OF BRAND PERSONALITY
P R E S C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
==PAGE:60==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   4 5
values. They challenged the team to broaden their 
view of what they were designing. The questions were 
therefore left unchanged in the second iteration.
The last task of placing the robot on scales was easy 
for the team to do. Faced with two extremes they had 
no trouble discussing and defining if the robot should 
be more one or the other or somewhere in-between, 
while the scales still facilitated a discussion on new 
facets of the robots personality. However the chosen 
scales were somewhat coincidental and not covering all 
the important aspects of personality. Even though they 
were taken from personality assessment tools, they 
were a collage of characteristics and not necessarily the 
whole story. Instead we took inspiration from The Big 
Five Personality Traits2 in making new headlines for the 
scales.
B e t t e r  f a c i l i t a t i o n 
The students in the second workshop had a great 
understanding of the idea and process of the method 
and the new facilitation and step-by-step approach was 
considered a success.
I T E R A T I O N  3
Iteration 3 of Robot Personality was a result of a 
discussion on the method as part of a toolbox during a 
workshop with 5 professional robotic developers.
During the third workshop the participants had very 
few comments for the pre-exercise describing brand 
personality. Some of the companies thought it very 
useful as a discussion tool to define their own or their 
customers brand personality, possibly together with 
their clients, others found it less important to the 
2  The Big Five consist of openness to experience, conscientousness, 
extraversion, agreeableness and neuroticism. They are high level traits 
of human personality generally agreed upon in personality research 
(Digman 1990)
development of HRI. As a pre-exercise we found that it 
could be presented as an optional tool, for those who 
found it useful. Very few and minor changes were made 
to the explanatory text on the template in the third 
iteration to be clearer on the purpose and procedure of 
the exercise.
The main exercise of defining the robots personality 
was very well received by the companies. They found 
it useful as an extension of the design specification 
and a tool to reach a common understanding of the 
expectations to a robot. Today they used similar, but 
less structured ways of determining the personality of 
the robot and were therefore happy to try the templates 
in their own development processes. As no real criticism 
were presented, the method and templates were left 
untouched in the third iteration.
5 . 1 . 5   M E T H O D  2  -  T H E 
Z O M B I E
As another way of incorporating anthropomorphic/
zoomorphic elements in a design the Zombie was 
developed. The purpose of the Zombie method was 
to introduce some lifelike elements in robots in their 
sound, movements and visual appearance. It has been 
seen that this is a way to enhance acceptance of robots 
by users (Duffy 2003). The concept was to let a design 
team choose a living being as their source of inspiration. 
This should be a being they wished to let their robot 
be associated with. The team should then define the 
characteristics of the being with respect to sound, 
movement and visual appearance (See the method  
template in figure 5–6). When the characteristics had 
been identified the team should simplify them down 
to their core. Last the team should exaggerate the 
simplified characteristics and incorporate them into 
solutions for their robot design. The output would be 
==PAGE:61==
4 6   |  
specific design suggestions for sounds, movements and 
visual appearance inspired from the living being. See 
appendix figure H–14.
Letting the designers be inspired from an actual living 
being would prime them to come up with ideas related 
to the sounds, movements and visual appearance of 
that being and thereby incorporate life-like elements 
into the robots design. Tseng, Moss, Cagan & Kotovsky 
(2008) has written about how the use of analogies as 
inspiration for design, can result in solutions related to 
that inspiration. With a starting point in a living being 
most of the ideas generated would be tied to that being. 
There is a danger in this method of being too fixated 
on the inspiration to come up with any other ideas, but 
if other brainstorming methods were used in the same 
development process, with a short break in-between, 
the fixation would wear off and open up for other 
solutions as well.
Using a living being as inspiration for the robot could 
also function as a reference point for the users and help 
them create the right expectations to the product. It has 
previously been seen how users treat a robot that looks 
like a dog similar to a dog (Paepcke & Takayama 2010) 
because it is their point of reference. This helps them 
to create a mental model for what the robot can do, 
and how to interact with it. Meanwhile the steps in the 
method are inspired from a method used by puppeteers 
(Jochum et al. 2014). Characterizing, simplifying and 
exaggerating movements from the real world is a way 
P R E S C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
figure 5–6.	THE ZOMBIE METHOD TEMPLATE
==PAGE:62==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   47
for them to identify core aspects of behaviour and use it 
in exaggerated form to infuse life into their puppets.
I T E R A T I O N  1
The first iteration of the Zombie was a result of testing 
the method on 4 students in a workshop on an 
imaginary case. During the workshop several problems 
were observed.
L a c k  o f  s e r i o u s n e s s 
The Zombie had evident challenges in the phrasing 
which gave the participants of the first workshop a 
hard time taking it seriously. The purpose and direct 
use of the method was not clear to them and this 
made a constructive discussion hard. Several ways of 
overcoming this problem were thought of, but were 
believed to devalue the method since the somewhat 
”crazy” point of entry to the design were the intended 
force of it.
N e e d  f o r  b a c k g r o u n d  k n o w l e d g e 
It became clear that a substantial basis of knowledge 
about the source of inspiration was very important for 
the successful use of the method. That would however 
demand some time to do research within the team 
or the help of an expert within the field of inspiration. 
If this was integrated it could make the method very 
similar to the more general Bio-Inspired Design Method 
described by Lenau, Cheong & Shu (2008) with a risk of 
loosing it’s novelty.
N o  u n i q u e  f o r c e s 
The Zombie had some direct similarities to the Robot 
Personality method in trying to guide the design 
towards anthropomorphic elements, where the Robot 
Personality solved the process and facilitation better. 
We do think the Zombie could be used to add life-
like animalistic feature to otherwise very mechanical 
designs, but it has some challenges as mentioned 
above. It does provide a source of inspiration for the 
designer, but has the disadvantage that the inspiration 
will easily be directly interpreted instead of at an 
abstract level, and will thereby limit the design instead 
of enhancing it. For these reasons we decided to stop 
the development of this method after the first workshop 
and instead focus on improving the Robot Personality 
that solved the same problem areas.
5 . 1 . 6   M E T H O D  3  -  C O N T E X T 
O F  T H E  R O B O T
The method Context of the Robot was created to 
accommodate for the problem of fitting a robot to it’s 
context and meanwhile develop a holistic product. 
The purpose of the Context of the Robot method 
was to create robotic products that could fit into their 
surroundings with a focus on the three dimensions 
sound, movement and visual appearance. This was done 
to create a frictionless domestication of the robot, as 
discovered in the research of e.g. Goetz et al. (2003) and 
Haring et al. (2013), where people prefered robots, that 
matched their task in looks and behaviour, and thereby 
confirmed the expectations to the robot. Further the 
use of well-known metaphors from the surroundings of 
the user are known in HCI to enhance usability (Marcus 
1998). The concept was to let a design team be inspired 
of video clips, sound recordings and pictures from the 
context during three separate brainstorms for their 
robotic design (see appendix figure H–19 for an example 
of the inspiration). During each of the brainstorms they 
should identify characteristics from the inspirational 
source, brainstorm on solutions that would not fit in and 
in the end turn those around into solutions that would 
fit the context. The output of the method would be ideas 
==PAGE:63==
4 8   |  
focusing on each of the three dimensions sound, motion 
and visual appearance of the robot. The ideas could be 
in the form of text, sketches, sound files, mock-ups etc.
The contextual inspiration would lead the design 
team’s ideas towards design elements well-known to 
the user as a function of priming. The method would 
put the designers mindset in the context of the user 
by providing inspiration from that environment. The 
method is based in the same theory as the Zombie 
with the use of analogies as inspiration by Tseng et al. 
(2008). With a starting point in the use context most of 
the ideas generated would be tied to that context.
The use of a negative brainstorm for each of the three 
dimensions had been included to diverge the ideas of 
the design team and help them analyse the context 
from a different perspective. Dix, Ormerod, Twidale, Sas, 
Alexandra & Mcknight (2006) claim that this is a great 
way enable novel ideation and to identify metrics with 
which to evaluate all ideas, why the outcome is not only 
silly ideas, but also a better understanding of the good 
solution.
Conducting three brainstorms with different foci would 
force a design team to think of all three aspects or 
a robot and not just the one they usually work with. 
Introducing self-imposed constrains, like the focus on 
context or the tasks of defining characteristics, bad 
and good solutions, has also been shown to increase 
creativity (Onarheim & Biskjaer 2014) and was seen 
in our interviews to be widely used by professional 
working with sound, movement and visual appearance. 
Utilizing the Context of the Robot method design teams 
could choose to use or disregard these constrains, 
according to other constraints imposed on the project, 
to reach, what Onarheim & Biskjaer (2014) calls the 
Sweetspot, where the team is neither over- or under 
constrained to reach as creative a state as possible.
I T E R A T I O N  1
The first iteration of Context of the Robot was the result 
of a test during a workshop with 5 students working 
on an imaginary case. We could see that the use of the 
method was very direct and that the participants had no 
trouble seeing the purpose and need of the method.
G r e a t  w i t h  d i v i s i o n 
The participants found the division of the three 
dimensions very usable, both as a step-by-step 
approach and to make sure to come around to all 
elements needed in the holistic design. We saw that the 
ideas created from this method was indeed inspired 
from and focused on making the product fit better into 
the context. The ideas also considered the indirect 
interaction between humans and robots that was not 
direct conversation, but more based on the expectations 
of what was found in a given environment.
M o r e  u s e  c a s e s 
Even though the method was introduced in the 
workshop as an early process step to turn user needs 
into a design, we saw from the workshop that it might 
be usable as well as a redesign tool to make sure a 
design would fit the context better.
S m a l l  c h a n g e s 
Since the method worked well the focus was on making 
it fit better into the process and as part of a whole. 
The three dimensions were gathered on one page 
to facilitate a holistic view of the robot and create 
an even more directed step-by-step feeling (See the 
development of the method in figure 5–7). 
From the first workshop we also found that this method 
might benefit from being used together with several 
creative apps and media like stop-motion film creators, 
P R E S C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
==PAGE:64==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   4 9
figure 5–7.	THE ITERATIONS OF CONTEXT OF THE ROBOT
==PAGE:65==
5 0   |  
GIF or sound apps to better facilitate brainstorming on 
sounds and movements. But since apps are quickly 
outdated we decided not to tie the method to any 
particular app and instead only mention a few and 
recommend the use of them or similar apps in the text 
accompanying the method.
To make the connection to the HRI clearer we decided to 
rephrase the headlines of the templates and provide the 
participants with a more clear introduction to the case. 
The result of iteration 1 of Context of the Robot can be 
seen in appendix figure H–16.
I T E R A T I O N  2
The second iteration of Context of the Robot was the 
result of a test during a workshop on an imaginary case 
by 4 students. This time as the second in a toolbox 
of four methods in succession. As in the in first the 
method was well-received by the students during the 
second workshop. Some of the issues that did appear 
were related to the formulation of the task.
A b s t r a c t  d e s c r i p t i o n s 
We had thought this method to be useful in discussing 
the overall guidelines for the sound, movements and 
visual appearance of the designed robot, but during 
the second workshop we witnessed how the team had 
difficulties discussing these dimensions at an abstract 
level. The participants would much rather have used the 
method to solve concrete problems on a more detailed 
level. This did not have implications for the design of 
the template, but for the suggested positioning of the 
method in a useflow.
L a c k  o f  s p a c e 
Another small issue that became apparent during 
the second workshop was the difficulty in keeping all 
characteristics and ideas visible throughout the use 
of the method. For the workshop the template had 
been printed in A3 format and identified characteristics 
and ideas were documented using post-it notes in 
three different colors. Because of the limited size 
of the template, the students had to first identify all 
characteristics, then move them outside the template 
to continue with creating bad ideas and later remove 
those to make room for good ideas. This created a 
break, where the students were often missing the 
overview of their previous work. To account for this we 
decided to recommend printing the template in A2 or 
bigger to make room for all post-its at once and added 
a division in characteristics, bad and good ideas on the 
template within each of the three categories to clearly 
communicate the intentions of the template containing 
the visual overview of the work done. The results of 
iteration 2 of Context of the Robot can be seen in 
appendix figure H–17.
I T E R A T I O N  3
Presented with the method of Context of the Robot 
the professionals participating in the third workshop 
were very excited. Several of them had experiences that 
robots that did not meet the users expectations had 
needed major design changes late in the development 
process. They saw a clear value in the division in the 
three dimensions and explained that this was similar 
to their usual way of working, but normally sound 
and movement was not considered. One participant 
suggested that this method could be used after the 
first meeting with a client to analyse and structure the 
gathered data.
Some of the suggestions that came up during the 
workshop was to add a dimension of what would a 
human, dog or other product do in the same situation. 
How would they sound, move and communicate 
P R E S C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
==PAGE:66==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   5 1
visually? Were there any good solutions on the marked 
already This would be a great addition to the method, 
but since it was very case-based this did not result 
in any changes to the template. Rather we would add 
these questions to the text accompanying the method 
as something to consider when using it.
The results of iteration 3 of Context of the Robot can be 
seen in appendix figure H–18.
5 . 1 . 7   M E T H O D  4  -  S C E N A R I O
Scenario was developed with a strength in creating 
understanding for the users’ perspective and testing 
out interactions quickly at low cost. The purpose of the 
Scenario method was to test out interactions between 
humans and robotic products in a quick and dirty 
fashion. This would make it possible to make faster 
alterations to the design earlier in the design process, 
before an expensive prototype had been created. 
The concept was to let a design team start out by 
brainstorming on different interactions that the robot 
they were developing might encounter. Second they 
would build a simple prototype of the robot or make 
a costume similar to it. The team should then walk 
through the different interaction scenarios several times 
in the roles of the involved actors. One of the team 
members should play the role of the robot. Between 
cycles the team could discuss improvement options for 
the robots behaviour according to sound, movement 
and visual appearance and write them down on a 
scenario card (See the development of the method 
in figure 5–8 and the first version in appendix figure 
H–20).
The concept was meant as a physical brainstorm based 
in basic Prototyping tools and Storyboarding techniques3 
3  Storyboarding in design is a technique to visualize a narrative about 
the use of a product in context (van Boeijen et al. 2014).
and would let the design team talk about the design 
while ”in character” as the user and robot exploring their 
reaction to the interaction on their own bodies. Using 
theatre methodologies to explore HRI has previously 
been used by both Hoffman (2011), IDEO (2014) and 
Christoffersen, Grinsted Nielsen, Jochum & Hua Tan 
(2015). Inspired by Pretotyping techniques4 this method 
was meant to help designers test out concepts fast and 
cheap, before having a working prototype.
I T E R A T I O N  1
The first iteration of Scenario was the result of a test 
during a workshop with 4 students working on an 
imaginary case. 
P r e - s t e p 
The first step in the Scenario method of identifying 
the interactions between the robot to be designed and 
humans in it’s environment worked well during the first 
workshop, which came across in the range of interaction 
situations found by the participants. The step did 
however not address the issue of specifying the details 
of the identified interactions. Once the team started 
role-playing, they had a bunch of unanswered questions 
about the actors involved, the time, the place etc. To 
improve on the outcome of the interaction brainstorm, 
we decided to extend it by first having an overall 
brainstorm, focusing on the interactions in a day in the 
life of the robot, followed by a detailing on the steps 
in each of the identified interactions. This was done 
to gain a clearer idea about each of the interactions, 
an overview of the alternative outcomes and be better 
prepared to act out the sequences. The pre-step was 
extracted as a separated method and prolonged to 
provide a better foundation to the physical brainstorm.
4  Pretotyping is a range of tools used to evaluate concepts before 
prototyping them using an element of make-believe (Savoia 2011).
==PAGE:67==
5 2   |  P R E S C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
figure 5–8.	THE ITERATIONS OF SCENARIO
==PAGE:68==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   5 3
E x t e n s i o n  o f  t h e  c a r d  n e e d e d 
The division into the three dimensions on the scenario 
card template gave the participants the possibility 
to discuss them in detail after each session, which 
provided a space for reflection within the group. During 
the session the group kept discussing the functions and 
features of the robot, which resulted in a fifth headline 
being added to the template describing this. On the 
scenario cards a user experience field was also added to 
let the team think more in the terms of action-reaction 
and thereby support the theory of successful interaction 
mentioned in the introduction of this report.
A p p o i n t e d  c r i t i c
Unexpectedly the participants had no problem acting 
out a Scenario and the method provided a lot of 
realisations connected to the interaction. There was 
however a need for a revision of the tool to investigate 
the ideas further. During the play-through of a Scenario 
it seemed to make sense to have an appointed critic on 
the team to ask questions that could facilitate further 
improvements of the ideas, like the role of ”the Black 
Hat” in the Six Thinking Hats5. The results of the first 
iteration of Scenario can be seen in appendix figure 
H–21.
I T E R A T I O N  2
The second iteration of Scenario was the result of a test 
during a workshop on an imaginary case by 4 students. 
This time the Interaction Mapping and Scenario were 
divided and presented as the third and fourth method in 
a toolbox of four methods used in succession. 
5  The Six Thinking Hats is a way to divide different perspectives among 
the participants in a meeting. The six imaginary hats each has a color 
and perspective associated with it. The white hat is focused on facts, the 
yellow hat is positive, the black hat is sceptical, the red hat is emotional, 
the green hat is creative, while the blue hat is focused on managing the 
session (de Bono 1985).
I n t e r a c t i o n  M a p p i n g 
During the second workshop it seemed that the 
extended version of the interaction brainstorm, where 
interactions were mapped on an overall and detailed 
level worked very well to give the designers an overview 
of the interactions they had to design for. They had far 
fewer questions for the Scenario once they started role-
playing. Still there were some room for improvements. 
As they started the exercise they had some trouble in 
figuring out where to begin. The template only showed 
a line and asked for normal and special interactions with 
no indication of where to start. To complete this task 
the students first had to decide, what a starting point 
might be and second think through the tasks of the 
robot and filter out those that contained interactions. 
Too much energy was spend doing this without any 
real value. In the second iteration we therefore added 
symbolic headlines to the time line saying ”The day 
starts by...” in the left side, and ”The day ends with...” 
in the right to ease the understanding. Additionally we 
changed the headline and nature of the task to mapping 
all events in a day in the life of the robot and not just 
the once containing interactions. The events containing 
interactions were then marked after the mapping to 
create focus points (see the development of the method 
in figure 5–9). 
At this point we also decided to separate the task of 
mapping interactions from the task of playing them out. 
Mapping the interactions could be useful even earlier on 
in the process, before the method Context of the Robot 
in the useflow.
S c e n a r i o 
For the role playing part of the method the students 
were surprised at how much they got out it. They 
applauded the idea of including the role of a critic to 
==PAGE:69==
5 4   |  P R E S C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
figure 5–9.	THE ITERATIONS OF INTERACTION MAPPING
==PAGE:70==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   5 5
better analyse the situation and question their actions. 
As an addition to the method it was discovered that 
having a person play the robot using their own body 
might be unfavourable as it made the other participants 
treat the ”robot” different than had it been a model. 
Instead we would recommend that a simple mock-up 
or cardboard box was controlled by one participant to 
simulate the robot. In that way the role-play would not 
be misleading because of subconscious human ways of 
communication. 
The scenario card template worked well in the second 
workshop and remained therefore unchanged.
I T E R A T I O N  3
The third iteration of Scenario was the result of a 
discussion with five professional robotic developers. 
Once again the Interaction Mapping and Scenario were 
presented seperately.
I n t e r a c t i o n  M a p p i n g 
During the third workshop we got the feedback from 
the participants that they were already using methods 
similar to the Interaction Mapping. They all had different 
names for them; Workflow, Usecase etc., but they were 
all about mapping the steps revolving the robot. For 
that reason they would probably keep using their own 
method and not convert to ours, even if Interaction 
Mapping was more focused on interactions. This 
proves that the method is indeed useful, but might not 
be needed by all, if they already employ alternative 
methods.
What was needed in the Interaction Mapping method 
was more details on, not only what events took place 
in a day in the life of the robot, but also who were 
involved, where it took place and why. By adding these 
questions to the template the method could to a greater 
extend supply design teams with the overview they 
needed (see appendix figure H–1 and figure H–2).
Further it was suggested in the third workshop that 
Personas could be used together with the map to 
predict different users reaction to different events 
and that the Scenario both before and after a robots 
implementation could be mapped to visualise the 
differences. These suggestions were added to the text 
accompanying the method as suggested ways of using 
it.
S c e n a r i o 
The Scenario method was well received by the 
participants in the third workshop. They saw great 
potential in acting out scenarios both together with 
users or as an earlier substitute for user testing, where 
concepts could be allowed to fail fast. A suggestion 
that came up during the workshop was to incorporate 
the rule that one member of the design team, that was 
not taking part in the role-play, could have the option 
to ask the role-players to stop acting to discuss the 
situation and then afterwords allow the them to move 
on. This could be used to ask the role-players to reflect 
on a particular situation and alternative possibilities. 
This suggestion was extended to the text accompanying 
the method. There was no critic of the scenario card 
template, why it remained unchanged during the third 
iteration.
5 . 2   C O M P O S I T I O N  O F 
T H E  H R I  T O O L B O X
During the exploratory studies we found that even 
though the developed methods were valuable 
individually they function more comprehensively in 
succession. The composition of the methods into a 
toolbox was first tested in the second workshop and 
==PAGE:71==
5 6   |  
was further developed and validated by the industry in 
the third workshop.
5 . 2 . 1   P O S I T I O N  O F 
T H E  H R I  T O O L B O X  I N  A 
D E V E L O P M E N T  P R O C E S S
In relation to a Stage Gate Model6, all of the methods 
in the toolbox have been created to help in the 
Development stage of a project, as this is where we 
saw a lack of methods in the industry (see figure 5–10). 
Here the standard design process is an iterative one 
made up by an Exploration phase, where new data is 
gathered, an Analysis phase, where the data is analysed 
and synthesised, an Ideation phase, where ideas and 
concepts for solutions are created, followed by a Build 
phase where the solutions are materialized in mock-
ups or prototypes, and in the end an Evaluation phase 
where the concepts are tested and evaluated (see 
appendix G).
S T A G I N G  O F  T H E  M E T H O D S
Through our workshops we saw the importance in 
staging and scoping of project tasks to have an efficient 
process and successful outcome of the methods. As 
described by Andreasen, Hansen & Cash (2015):
”Staging builds on, and helps shape, a team’s shared 
understanding, values, and insights. As such, it is a 
key element in any community of practice. Core to 
this is developing the link between a team and its 
surroundings to support both awareness and insight.”
Being in the right mindset, with the right people 
at the right place or knowing the goals of a task is 
crucial for productivity. During our first workshop 
6  The Stage Gate Model has here been chosen as the example of 
a development process since it was well-known to the interviewed 
companies and easily understood as a reference. 
the students were thrown into different task, without 
much introduction or preparation, which resulted in 
confusion and frustration. During the second workshop 
we therefore added a warm-up exercise together with 
a pre-exercise to the method Robot Personality to 
prepare them for the kind of work they would be doing 
and extended introductions about the mechanisms and 
goals of each task. It was clear to us that the outcome 
of the later workshops was significantly improved on 
this account. We therefore ended up included a scoping 
meeting in the beginning of the suggested useflow of 
the toolbox to encourage the planning of task staging 
and scoping prior to the use of the methods. This could 
work as a meeting between collaborators discussing the 
desired outcome of the project to make sure everybody 
was on board and agreed on the goals.
E X P L O R A T I O N  P H A S E
In the workshops we experienced some frustration 
from the participants due to a lack of knowledge on 
the given cases. To be able to solve the task at hand 
they felt a need to understand the situation in detail; 
who was involved, what were they like, where did it 
take place etc.. We accounted for this by incorporating 
the recommendation to do general research of the 
users and their needs, the context and market, early in 
the useflow, before using the methods, to inform the 
decisions made. This could be done in the Exploration 
phase of the process. 
A N A LY S I S  P H A S E
In the Analysis phase the team of designers that have 
been exploring the Context of the Robot can use the 
Interaction Mapping method to synthesis the gathered 
data and get an overview of the robot’s tasks and 
interactions. This could be done internally in the team 
or as a workshop tool together with the clients or users. 
P R E S C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
==PAGE:72==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   5 7
Earlier in the project this method was placed later in the 
process together with the Scenario method, but it was 
discovered during the second workshop and confirmed 
during the third that the outcome of the method could 
also be useful as an input to the other methods.
I D E A T I O N  P H A S E
The Brand Personality method was created as a pre-
exercise to Robot Personality to give the design team 
a clear starting point that not only would help them to 
achieve the right mindset and start seeing inanimate 
objects as having a personality, but also force them to 
create a clear vision for the product line. That is why 
it is placed immediately before the Robot Personality 
method in the Ideation phase, even if it is more of an 
analysis tool. Early on we thought of these methods as 
some of the first to be used in the useflow as they could 
help define the goal with the robot, but we later realized 
that an understanding and analysis of the context was 
needed before these goals could be set. In addition we 
discovered that Robot Personality was in fact a ideation 
tool that could help create the personality of a robot.
In the Ideation phase the contexts is also dragged upon 
to inspire solutions that will create easier domestication 
with the Context of the Robot method. The position of 
the method was always very clear as an ideation tool 
and was only confirmed during the workshops. It could 
help in producing interaction design solutions within 
sound, movement and appearance familiar to- and 
fulfilling the expectations of users of robotic products.
figure 5–10.	
THE HRI TOOLBOX INCORPORATED INTO A GENERIC STAGEGATE MODEL
==PAGE:73==
5 8   |  
B U I L D  A N D  E V A L U A T I O N  P H A S E
In the Build and Evaluation phase of the process the 
team could put the last method into use. With the 
Scenario method the team could test out interaction 
scenarios quickly and effectively without spending 
the time and money to create a working prototype. 
This method could be used internally in the team as a 
preliminary test of concepts or in workshops with the 
users in the field. As we learned in our third workshop, 
the time with clients and users is often limited. The 
value of testing the scenarios out prior to evaluating 
with them is therefore high, so that the time at the 
scene can be used as effectively as possible.
P R E S C R I P T I V E  ST U D I E S
==PAGE:74==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   5 9
