6 . 1   T H E  N E E D
More and more products are becoming robotic in 
their form. Computers are incorporated everywhere to 
support smart autonomous behaviours and facilitate 
mobility as seen in robotic lawnmowers, cars etc. With 
this development and the maturing of technology, a 
focus on HRI becomes ever more important. As the 
functionality of a robot becomes more complicated, 
there is a risk of the interaction becoming more 
complicated too, to a point where nobody can 
understand it. A robotic product that can function, 
but that no one can control is not much use and will 
not become a success. Really companies should not 
just strive for usable robots, but robots creating great 
user experiences that will set them apart from their 
competition and be an important selling point.
Today there is a lack of methods to take on the 
challenge of designing HRI. A few researchers have 
begun to experiment in this area and document their 
experiences, but few methods usable in industrial 
product development have yet come out of it. In the 
robotic industry the methods used have appeared 
through experience or have been borrowed from 
normal product development or HCI design methods, 
leaving the process unstructured or some dimensions 
of the robotic presence and communication 
unaccounted for. Through interviews with professionals 
from four different Danish companies developing 
robotic products, we have identified six problem areas, 
where no systematic development methods reflected 
current research findings in HRI. The six areas were
•	
Managing user expectations in the interaction 
with robots
•	
Incorporating anthropomorphic elements in 
designs
•	
Fitting robots to their context 
•	
Creating holistic design concepts, guiding all 
dimensions of design 
•	
Understanding the perspective of users in their 
meeting with a robot 
T H I S  C H A P T E R  W O R K S  A S  A  STA N D  A LO N E  PA P E R  E X P L A I N I N G  T H E  N E E D 
F O R - ,  D O G M A S - ,  M E T H O D S - ,  A N D  U S E F LO W  O F  T H E  F I N A L  T O O L B OX  F O R  H R I 
D ES I G N .  E A C H  O F  T H E  M E T H O D S  W I L L  B E  E X P L A I N E D  I N  D E P T H  A S  W E L L 
A S  T H E  I N T E N D E D  S U C C E S S I O N  A N D  I N T E G R AT I O N  W I T H  A  D E V E LO P M E N T 
P R O C ES S .  T H R O U G H O U T  T H E  C H A P T E R  A N  E X A M P L E  O F  A  H A R D WA R E  ST O R E 
A S S I STA N T  R O B O T  I S  U S E D  T O  P R OV I D E  A  B E T T E R  U N D E R STA N D I N G  O F  T H E 
U S E  O F  T H E  M E T H O D S .
THE HRI DESIGN 
TOOLBOX
==PAGE:77==
62   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
62   |  
•	
Testing the interaction with a robot before 
reaching a full prototype 
Through this project we have discovered a need for a 
toolbox of methods covering these areas in order for 
the industry to be able to create great user experiences 
through design for HRI.
6 . 2   T H E  H R I  T O O L B O X
The HRI Toolbox is a collection of design methods 
targeted for social and service robots that share an 
environment with humans. It deals with problems 
in the development phase, which can have crucial 
effects on the final robotic product, such as misuse, 
loss of interest, unpredicted mishaps, expectations 
not being met or a general lack of integration into the 
environment.
The HRI Toolbox consists of four methods; Robot 
Personality (which also includes a short staging 
exercise), Interaction Mapping, Context of the Robot 
and Scenario. A suggested positioning of the methods 
in a generic Stagegate Model can be seen in figure 
6–1. The methods can be used either in the suggested 
succession or be hand-picked to suit the needs in a 
team. The useflow of the methods is elaborated further 
in section 6.3. We do suggest, if all of the methods are 
not being used, that other methods are utilized to tackle 
the problems they each address.
The HRI Toolbox is being presented together with a 
list of dogmas that are important considerations when 
figure 6–1.	THE HRI TOOLBOX INCORPORATED INTO A GENERIC STAGEGATE MODEL
==PAGE:78==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   6 3
designing for HRI. These should be taken into account 
and in the ideal case be hung so that they are visible to 
the development team all through a project.
6 . 2 . 1   T H E  D O G M A S  O F  H R I
1 .  M A N A G I N G  P E R C E I V E D 
A F F O R D A N C E  I S  K E Y 
A clear communication of what a product is able 
to do and how it functions is the most important 
consideration to make in a development phase. Does 
your design clearly communicate the abilities of your 
product? Does your design behave according to your 
users expectations?
An example of a robotic product with a very clear 
communication of its abilities is the BeamPro 
telepresence robot (see figure 6–2). The only elements 
it has is a screen, a camera and wheels, which makes it 
clear that it can show something, film the user and drive 
around. There is no need to provide it with a humanoid 
body or the like, since it will only mislead the perceived 
affordance.
2 .  U S E  C O M M O N  R E F E R E N C E S , 
M E T A P H O R S  A N D  C O N V E N T I O N S 
You do not need to reinvent the wheel every time 
you make a decision. Our internal library of common 
references is very useful. Think of what else is common 
knowledge in the field where the product needs to 
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   6 3
figure 6–2.	 THE TELEPRESENCE ROBOT BEAM PRO CLEARLY 
COMMUNICATES ITS ABILITIES (BEAMPRO N.D.).
figure 6–3.	THE 3DR SMART SOLO DRONE HAS REFERENCES TO CAR 
DESIGNS WITH WHITE FRONT AND RED REAR LIGHTS (3DR SOLO N.D.).
==PAGE:79==
6 4   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
figure 6–4.	THE JAPANESE ROBOT PEPPER IS TO BE RELEASED IN THE US WITH MODIFICATIONS, MAKING IT FIT THE NEW ENVIRONMENT 
(PEPPER N.D.).
figure 6–5.	JIBO IS AN EXAMPLE OF A ROBOT UTILIZING LIMITED 
ANTHROPOMORPHIC ELEMENTS IN IT’S DESIGN (JIBO N.D.).
figure 6–7.	THE GOOGLE SELFDRIVING CAR IS MODIFIED TO BE MORE HUMAN IN ITS REACTION PATTERNS 
MAKING IT MORE PREDICTABLE AND LESS CAUTIOUS (GOOGLE SELF-DRIVING CAR PROJECT N.D.).
figure 6–6.	STARSHIP’S SELF DRIVING DELIVERY ROBOT LOOKS LIKE A 
LITTLE BUG AND IS VERY ”RESPECTFUL” TOWARDS HUMAN PEDESTRIANS 
(STARSHIP N.D.).
==PAGE:80==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   6 5
operate. There are a lot of unspoken references and 
conventions in hospitals, schools, the street etc.
When designing communication of robot navigation for 
example, the reference to regular headlights from cars 
are known to most of the world and can be usable in 
robotic designs as well. This is used by the 3DR Smart 
Solo Drone (see figure 6–3), that has small lights to 
show the direction of its flight.
3 .  B E  A W A R E  O F  C U LT U R A L 
D I F F E R E N C E S 
The robot you develop will not be interpreted the same 
all over the world. Cultural references and social norms 
are different and peoples interpretations can cause 
unexpected reactions to certain greetings, intonations, 
colors etc.. It is important to be aware of norms in the 
cultural context your robot must function in and the 
markets it is expected to be released in. The culture 
might be related to a country, workplace, generation or 
other. A new HRI design might be necessary, if you are 
planning to release your robot in a new country or for a 
different environment.
An example of this is the Japanese robot Pepper that 
will release in the US in 2016. On that account the 
design team has made some modifications to how it 
approaches the users (see figure 6–4). In the Japanese 
version the robot had a very cute and high pitched 
voice and were programmed to play hide-n-seek with 
the users to cheer them up. In the American version it 
is snarky and smart and have been programmed to fist 
bump and talk back to the users, which the team hopes 
will be the key to success in the American market.
4 .  U S E  A N T H R O P O M O R P H I C 
E L E M E N T S  W I T H  C A R E
Anthropomorphic elements such as mimicking face 
muscle or gaze are often seen as helpful in social 
communication, but in any robotic product the amount 
of anthropomorphic elements must be carefully 
considered, since they have a direct influence on the 
perceived affordance of the product. Too little makes 
a robot seem stupid and dead, too much can make a 
user uncomfortable or disappointed with the actual 
abilities of the robot. Ask yourself: How could your robot 
be more anthropomorphic? What happens to the users 
expectations? How could it be less anthropomorphic? 
Does this communicate the products true functionality?
An interesting example is the social robot Jibo, that 
even though it is shaped by geometrical figures is still 
perceived very human in its movements and graphic 
animations (see figure 6–5). The restricted two degrees 
of freedom made by the inclined movable elements 
makes the movements smooth and lifelike. The constant 
feedback from the animation on the screen provides the 
user with gaze-like behaviour, which also curves when 
the robot is laughing to simulate the wrinkles by the 
eyes of a human.
5 .  B E  A W A R E  O F  F E A R  O F  R O B O T S 
It is very important to be aware of and consider the 
tendency to fear of robotic products in the public. It is 
something that a developers should consider in the 
shaping and conceptualisation of a robot, since the 
user must be helped to accept and understand the 
use and limits of the products. The Kindchenschema1 
has previously been used in robotic designs to avoid 
this fear and it can also be considered that humanoids 
are generally more accepted in social tasks, while 
1  Read about Kindchenschema used in e.g. Jibo here http://www.
spektrum.de/lexikon/biologie/kindchenschema/36103
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   6 5
==PAGE:81==
6 6   |  
mechanical robots are more accepted for non-social 
tasks.
A company that is coming out with a self driving 
delivery robot in 2016 has taken this paradigm into 
account in its design. Starship is shaped like a little 
bug or toy car, which with the low hight and light colors 
makes it non-threatening to the pedestrians in it’s way. 
Whenever a pedestrian is near the robot politely drives 
backwards up the nearest driveway and greats (see 
figure 6–6).
6 .  T H E  I N T E N T I O N S  O F  A  R O B O T 
M U S T  B E  P R E D I C T A B L E  A N D 
R E A D A B L E 
To help users of a robot it is important to communicate 
the robot’s intentions. Think about the predictability - 
How can a user predict what the robot is going to do? 
- and legibility of the robot - How can a user know what 
the robot is currently doing? The use of the two must 
be understood and designed for. To integrate intentions 
more clearly in a robot’s behaviour look e.g. into the 
12 principles of animation2, gazing, feedback sounds, 
responses, anthropomorphic movement, etc..
In designing their self driving car Google found that a lot 
of the actions done by the car, especially in situations 
where it were about to turn, were way too careful (see 
figure 6–7). This made the car very unpredictable to 
pedestrians and annoying to the driver of the car. The 
car would for example break if anybody went near 
the edge of the pavement, even though they had no 
intentions of crossing the road. They are therefore 
currently redesigning its reaction patterns to be more 
”human” in its movements without making it less safe.
2  To see them in action go to: http://the12principles.tumblr.com
6 6   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
==PAGE:82==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   67
6 . 2 . 2   M E T H O D  1  -  I N T E R A C T I O N  M A P P I N G
The Interaction Mapping method combines the mapping of events and alternative outcomes of interactions to 
create awareness of how a day in the life of the robot goes by.
P U R P O S E 
Interaction Mapping is used in the end of the 
exploration phase or the beginning of the analysis 
phase to create a bridge between the research done 
in the field and the creative design phases. It can help 
develop better user experience by predict and map each 
possible interaction. This method gives the designers 
an overview of the use phase of the robot with a special 
focus on the interactions between the robot and users, 
the objective of each interaction and how they fit into 
context.
P R E P A R A T I O N S 
To be able to utilize the full potential of the method a 
thorough study of the scene the robot must act in and 
the users it must interact with is important preparations. 
The best inputs for the method is pictures from the 
scene, personas of the expected users and ideas 
regarding the tasks of the robot.
S T A G I N G 
The method will be able to work as a boundary object 
with the templates as a communication tool between 
field researchers and the design team. They should both 
meet in a workshop and let the template be plotted as 
a big poster on the wall for everybody to work around. 
The ideas and interaction brainstorm should be as free 
as possible, it is important not to discard any ideas at 
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   67
figure 6–8.	 INTERACTION MAPPING TEMPLATE 1 AND 2
==PAGE:83==
6 8   |  
once. Later a prioritization session will help determine 
which interactions should be focused on.
H O W  T O  U S E  T H E  M E T H O D 
The method is twofold; a map of the entire day maps 
events on an overall level (See figure 6–8), while an 
in-depth description of each interaction maps the detail. 
First the team brainstorms on which events the robot 
would encounter during a normal day, writes each event 
on a post-it and places them horizontally in the top 
row in order of occurrence. When all the normal events 
have been covered, special events, like repairs, extreme 
cases etc., are filled out in the next row. When the team 
cannot think of any more events, the ones that involves 
an interaction with a person is highlighted with a circle, 
to make them stand out. It should here be discussed 
how the interactions should be prioritized if there is not 
enough time to handle all of them. The goal is to have 
a clear overview of each interaction, so a prioritization 
is not a reason to discard them, but simply to be sure 
that the most important/common/critical interactions 
are dealt with first. After this the users the robot is 
interacting with, the place it occurs and why a robot 
is needed for such a task, is filled in for each of the 
interactive events. This is where the preparation from 
the field team is key. The user description should be a 
short persona that contains the most basic information 
to be used as a reference tool and a discussion piece. 
The place is either described, drawn or even better 
photographed to be sure that the whole team is aware 
of and agrees upon the specific place of the interaction. 
The why can be used to make a quick back-of-the-
envelope calculation of why a robot is best for the task. 
Is there for example an increased level of costumer 
service? Can a robot do the task faster/better/cheaper? 
This field on the template can help keep the objective of 
the interaction in mind.
On the second template each of the interactions from 
the first template are further explored. This step is 
done for each of the interactions or if time is short for 
the highest prioritized. A step by step flow diagram 
of the interaction is made and all possible alternative 
course of events included. Different colours of post-its 
can be used to differentiate between the actions of the 
user and robot. When going through these individual 
interactions the team should transfer the notes from the 
first template on how many are involved, who they are, 
what the surroundings look like and why this interaction 
is important.
S U G G E S T E D  P R O C E D U R E
1.	
Fill in events occurring for the robot during a 
regular day in the top horizontal row of template 
1.
2.	
Fill in special events in the second row, e.g. 
repairs, charging, vandalism, etc. 
3.	
Highlight the events that are interactions with 
humans.
4.	
Fill in Place, Users and Why? for each interaction.
5.	
Transfer one of the interactions with 
appertaining Users, Place and Objective to 
template 2.
6.	
Create a flow diagram for the interaction with 
alternative directions of the scenario.
7.	
Repeat step 5 and 6 for each interaction.
T I P S ,  T R I C K S  A N D  C O N C E R N S 
Alternatively events involving interactions with other 
smart technologies can also be marked on the map to 
identify situations of particular focus for the technical 
6 8   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
==PAGE:84==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   69
figure 6–9.	EXAMPLE OF A FILLED OUT INTERACTION MAPPING TEMPLATE 1 AND 2 FOR A HARDWARE STORE ROBOT
==PAGE:85==
7 0   |  
development; must the robot e.g. interact with elevators, 
other robots or an internet database
The Interaction Mapping method has the risk of being 
based on assumptions about the robots tasks and 
interactions, why the outcome of the first iteration of 
this can be imperfect. It is therefore important that it 
is updated when new knowledge is retrieved or used 
together with users to get accurate informations.
O U T C O M E 
The goal of this method is to make sure there are no 
use scenarios in the intended field unaccounted for. 
When this method is done successfully the team should 
have a general overview of all the interaction and a 
detailed understanding of at least the most important/
common/critical interactions ready for the design 
team to use in the Context of the Robot and Scenario 
methods.
E X A M P L E 
If you were to design a robot for a hardware store to 
help customers locate what they are looking for, some 
of the identified events could be e.g. robot is turned 
on, maps the store, detects customer, helps customer 
etc., while special event might be calling for repair or 
running out of power. The users in these interaction 
could be employees with different degree of technical 
expertise, technicians for repair or customers with 
different personas and the place specific locations like 
the entrance, stock room or store floor. The objective of 
the interaction is placed in the bottom row and could 
for example be that the store wants to save power 
during the night, why the robot needs to be switched 
on/off before and after opening hours. An example of 
a filled out template 1 is seen in figure 6–9. To further 
investigate the interaction of turning on the robot, the 
users, place and objective for this interaction is moved 
down to the second template. Here the scenario starts 
out with the robot being off, an employee arrives at 
work, goes to the robot, turns it on etc. Alternatives 
can be explored where relevant. If the robot is switched 
on there could for instance be one scenario, where it 
does not have enough power and one where it is fully 
powered. Here it can be explored how that changes the 
interaction.
L I M I T A T I O N S  A N D  A LT E R N A T I V E S 
As we learned during our third workshop this method 
is already commonly deployed in many companies 
in various forms. If you are already using or familiar 
with any of these they might be the better choice for 
you. Similar to our method is among others Customer 
Journeys, Storyboarding, Written Scenarios described 
by e.g. van Boeijen et al. (2014) and Interaction 
Experience Maps  described by Young et al. (2011) 
that might be used instead. It is important which ever 
method is used to keep the main objective in focus, 
that all the interactions are mapped out, not just the 
technical perimeters but also the human feedback and 
the context of the space.
7 0   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
==PAGE:86==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   7 1
6 . 2 . 3   M E T H O D  2  -  R O B O T  P E R S O N A L I T Y
The Robot Personality method lets you think about the robot as more than an object to create a more holistic and 
lifelike product. This is done by opening up for a personality to be the background for the behaviour of the robot 
and by using the final profile as a guideline in the ongoing development.
P U R P O S E 
The Robot Personality method, along with the Brand 
Personality pre-exercise, is used as one of the first 
steps in the ideation phase to create an understanding 
in the team of what kind of robot is being developed. 
The integration of a personality has shown to increase 
long term interactions with users and thereby create a 
better user experience, while the integration of brand 
profile makes the brand recognizable across products 
and aligns them with the company vision.
P R E P A R A T I O N S 
The method Robot Personality doesn’t require any 
direct preparations, but a clear understanding of the 
context, idea behind the robot and the user is valuable, 
such as the one obtained in the Interaction Mapping. 
For the pre-exercise Brand Personality the team should 
revisit the vision and mission of the company for it to be 
valuable and efficient.
S T A G I N G 
The method should be facilitated as two subsequent 
workshops. The first doing the pre-exercise with 
the design team and preferably also the company 
leadership represented. The second working through 
the Robot Personality template only with the design 
team. The leadership should be there in the beginning 
to ensure a correct understanding of the brand profile 
and discuss the strategy of if and how directly the brand 
should be reflected in the design of the robot. This 
exercise not only lets the team pin down what the brand 
figure 6–10.	
BRAND PERSONALITY AND ROBOT PERSONALITY TEMPLATES
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   7 1
==PAGE:87==
7 2   |  
wants to portray in their product line, but also gets 
them into the mindset of thinking of inanimate objects 
as having a personality. In the second workshop it is 
necessary to have a more relaxed atmosphere where 
nobody is afraid to contribute, why the leadership is 
best left out of this session.
H O W  T O  U S E  T H E  M E T H O D 
The Robot Personality begins with the pre-exercise; 
Brand Personality, that lets the participants discuss 
the personality profile of the brand for whom they 
are designing (see figure 6–10). A pool of personality 
characteristics is presented on the template along with 
blank notes, where the team can write any additional 
characteristics they feel are missing. The team 
discusses the characteristics one by one in relation 
to the brand and identifies and places the five most 
descriptive personality characteristics in the red circle to 
convey their conclusion.
The main task in Robot Personality uses three steps to 
design a point of origin for a descriptive personality to 
be used as a concept for a robot (see figure 6–10). The 
first step is an unlimited brainstorm on ideal personality 
characteristics for the robot that lets the team approach 
the ultimate persona for the task. This is followed by 
step two that contains a number of questions to make 
sure the team has gotten around to the deeper trades 
of the personality, which creates more interesting 
interactions. In the last step the team points out the 
personality of the robot on five scales to compare it to 
other personalities.
S U G G E S T E D  P R O C E D U R E
0.	
Identify the five most descriptive characteristics 
of the brand personality and place them within 
the red circle.
T h e  m a i n  m e t h o d
1.	
Brainstorm freely on characteristics of the ideal 
personality to fulfil the task. 
2.	
Discuss and answer the follow up questions one 
by one.
3.	
Place the robot’s personality on the five scales 
at the bottom of the template.
T I P S ,  T R I C K S  A N D  C O N C E R N S 
Try not to thinking about traditional or existing robot 
personalities when using the method. There is a risk 
of constraining the design unnecessarily. Think instead 
of the ideal personality for the job, what type of person 
would the users feel comfortable working with in this 
situation.
O U T C O M E 
The direct take away from this process is the filled 
out template on which the personality is described. 
This should be hung so that it is visible to the team 
throughout the project and used as a reference when 
considering design solutions. All three steps in the 
template should be completed in the workshop, but 
can be refined during several iterations when new 
realisations emerge.
E X A M P L E 
Using the same example as previous, Silvan is a 
Danish hardware store that might be needing a robot 
to help guide customers in their purchase. Their vision 
is ”SILVAN is the absolute most service-oriented DIY 
hardware store, when you want to decorate, repair or 
maintain your residence.” Utilizing the Brand Personality 
exercise some of the most descriptive words they 
would wish to identify with would be tough, reliable and 
7 2   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
==PAGE:88==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   7 3
figure 6–11.	
EXAMPLE OF FILLED OUT BRAND PERSONALITY AND ROBOT PERSONALITY TEMPLATES
==PAGE:89==
74   |  
down-to-earth, while upper class and daring are less 
descriptive. Some words that might also be needed are 
handy and easy-to-talk-to that can be written on some 
of the blank notes of the template. The words are cut 
out from the template, placed in the red circle and hung 
in the work area of the design team. An example of the 
used template can be seen in figure figure 6–11.
Moving on to the Robot Personality template the ideal 
personality characteristics for the robot can be written 
directly on the template or on post-its for greater 
flexibility. The characteristics could be words such as 
friendly, wise, robust and warm. A personality like an 
older handyman calm and knowledgeable in the field, 
someone the customers can trust for advice on their 
DIY projects. It is motivated by helping and values good 
quality over price. If the customers interact with it for 
a while it might attempt a joke and it will remember 
them and their projects the next time they enter 
the store. Assessed on the scales of the template it 
should be more curious than cautious, somewhere in 
between efficient and easy-going, outgoing, friendly and 
confident. When filled out the template is hung beside 
the Brand Personality in the work area to consult when 
designing. As the sounds are designed for instance, 
they can be compared to the described personality to 
see if they align. In this case deeper base sounds might 
better reflect the personality than high pitched beeping. 
An example of a filled out Robot Personality template 
can be seen in figure 6–11.
L I M I T A T I O N S  A N D  A LT E R N A T I V E S 
The pre-exercise in the method Robot Personality is an 
adaptation of Jennifer Aaker’s categorization of brand 
personality (Aaker 1997). Our version was designed to 
be a short exercise that could bring up a discussion 
of brand personality. It is not meant to facilitate an 
in-depth analysis of the brand, but as a short reminder 
to get in the right mindset before designing the robots 
personality. If a longer and more in depth version of 
that exercise is wanted the original design might be 
favourable to use.
The main exercise of creating a personality for the robot 
is meant to help generate some abstract guidelines 
for the design of the robots behaviour. The limitations 
of this method is that it will not always provide a clear 
answer to any design decisions, rather a guideline to 
the preferred direction. As an alternative to this method 
an ordinary persona template might be used on the 
robot or an extended design specification, like the one 
described by Cross (2008), could capture the goals 
for the robot personality. The frameworks of Bartneck 
& Forlizzi (2004) or Goodrich & Schultz (2007) could 
also be used as a point of discussion for the robots 
characteristics related to other robots, which the current 
method does not. Finally we heard from one company 
that they developed a mascot or logo for the robot, 
before designing the real thing. This could also be a way 
to process the goals for the robot personality before 
designing it (see appendix A.2.3). 
74   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
==PAGE:90==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   7 5
M E T H O D  3  -  C O N T E X T  O F  T H E  R O B O T
Context of the Robot lets a development team use their research gathered in the field as a point of origin for the 
creative concept development. Here the looks, sounds and appearance of the setting as well as the social norms 
of the context help to make the robotic product fit into its surroundings, and thereby contribute to an easier 
domestication.
P U R P O S E 
The Context of the Robot is a method created to 
help the team get a clear and united perception of 
the intended field of use for the robot and utilize it in 
creating suitable concepts. This method should be done 
in the beginning of the ideation phase and is a great 
way to use some of the material collected during the 
Exploration phase of the project, like pictures, film clips, 
etc.
P R E P A R A T I O N S 
The preparations for this methods lies in the field 
research and consists of pictures, film- and sound 
clips from the intended surroundings of the robot. If no 
sound clips have been made the soundtrack to the film 
clips should be extracted as a separate file to make sure 
the dimensions are considered individually.
S T A G I N G 
The method should be facilitated as a workshop for the 
design team and field researchers. In the brainstorming 
sessions every team member should have their own 
pen and post-its to make sure everybody speak their 
mind and provide as many ideas as possible while 
speaking out loud. Start by setting a time limit to each 
step to make the work flow faster.
figure 6–12.	
CONTEXT OF THE ROBOT  TEMPLATE
==PAGE:91==
76   |  
H O W  T O  U S E  T H E  M E T H O D 
The method works in three similar steps, with each 
three sub-steps. For the template see figure 6–12. First 
the surrounding sounds are investigated by listening to 
sound clips captured in the future environment of the 
robot. This could be the sound part of a recorded film or 
small sound bits recorded with a dictaphone. Based on 
the sound clips a brainstorm is executed in the design 
team, first focusing on the significant characteristics 
of the sounds in the environment. Next the team goes 
through a negative brainstorm, coming up with ideas for 
sounds that would not fit into this environment. Lastly 
these are turned into a positive brainstorm to come 
up with solutions that does fit the sound scape of the 
place. These three brainstorms are then repeated within 
movements and appearances of the surroundings. Here 
films and images from the research phase can be used 
as inspiration sources.
S U G G E S T E D  P R O C E D U R E
1.	
Listen to sound clips from the context
a.	
Brainstorm on what significant 
characteristics the sounds have
b.	
Brainstorm on which sound would not fit into 
this soundscape
c.	
Turn the brainstorm around to what would 
fit in
2.	
Watch film clips from the context
a.	
Brainstorm on what significant 
characteristics the movement have in the 
surroundings
b.	
Brainstorm on which movements would not 
fit into this character scheme
c.	
Turn the brainstorm around to what would 
fit in
3.	
Look at a collage or moodboard of the context 
images
a.	
Brainstorm on what significant 
characteristics the appearance have  
b.	
Brainstorm on what would not fit into this 
character scheme
c.	
Turn the brainstorm around to what would 
fit in
T I P S ,  T R I C K S  A N D  C O N C E R N S 
It is recommended to integrate sound and motion 
smart-phone and tablet apps into the brainstorming 
process to be able to quickly explore and communicate 
sound and motion ideas (App examples could be 
Autodesk Force Effect Motion, Stop Motion Studio, or 
Animation Creator HD Express for movements, and 
Keezy or Beatwave for sound).
If the method is used to come up with solutions for a 
specific interaction with a robot it can also be useful 
to take one round to brainstorm on how others act in 
the given situation for inspiration. How would a human, 
dog, other product etc. sound, move and appear in this 
interaction.
O U T C O M E 
The goal of this method is to come up with ideas and 
concepts for the HRI design of a robot, focusing on a 
smooth transitioning period for the user by creating 
awareness of and letting the team be inspired by the 
surrounding movement, sound and appearance and 
thereby the subtle expectations of the user. The method 
can be used both on a overall level and as a more detail 
focused brainstorm by going into each function or 
interaction of the robot. In that case the outcome should 
be more specific and the ideas ready to be used in e.g. 
76   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
==PAGE:92==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   7 7
a Morphology Chart1 or similar to be sure the entire field 
is explored.
E X A M P L E 
The hardware store robot will need to fit into the context 
of the Silvan stores while embracing the personality trait 
of being outgoing, taken from the Robot Personality 
method. Material has been collected in the exploration 
phase and the team have brought a film of a typical 
customer journey in Silvan, sound clips from a trip 
1  A Morphology Chart is a way to structure sub-solutions in functions 
and combine them into new holistic solutions (Cross 2008).
through the store and a moodboard with images from 
the store, the product selection and the visual vision. 
During the sound brainstorm the noises recollected in 
the sound clip from Silvan could be background noise, 
metallic sounds, talk and generally loudness. This 
would make sounds like jazz, soft tones and whisper 
inappropriate and unbecoming. By having the Robot 
Personality in mind the team now turns the Negative 
Brainstorm into sounds fitting for the personality of the 
brand and robot as well as the context in general, e.g. 
deep, but fairly round and loud sounds. Afterwards the 
figure 6–13.	
CONTEXT OF THE ROBOT  TEMPLATE EXAMPLE
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   7 7

brainstorms are done for movement and appearance 
based on the remaining material. The example can be 
seen in figure 6–13.
L I M I T A T I O N S  A N D  A LT E R N A T I V E S 
The Context of the Robot method is a brainstorming 
tool like so many others. It demands some time for 
preparation, to collect pictures, sound and film clips 
as the source of inspiration, all though we have found 
the majority of design teams to do this already. As 
mentioned earlier there are some risk of getting fixated 
on the inspirational source, when using this method, 
why it is important to conduct additional brainstorming 
sessions using other methods to be sure to cover the 
whole solution space. If this method should not fit your 
process, be too restrictive or not restrictive enough, 
many others can be used instead. Incorporated into 
the method is a negative brainstorm that could also 
be used on it’s own for fewer restrictions. Otherwise 
analogy brainstorms2 or Bio-inspired design methods3, 
might be the best suited alternative to come up with 
good solutions fitted to the context.
2  Analogy brainstorms is a method where designers are inspired from 
things analogous to the products they want to design. The analogies 
can be direct, personal, symbolic, fantasy or other (Cross 2008)
3  Bio-inspired design is a method where designers use inspiration 
from natures solution to similar problems to create new solutions for 
products (Lenau 2008).
78   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
==PAGE:94==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   7 9
6 . 2 . 4   M E T H O D  4  -  S C E N A R I O
Scenario is a physical brainstorming tool that lets a design team try out different versions of an interaction to 
foresee any obvious and non-obvious clashes between the robot and human user. Later in the process the Scenario 
tool can be used as a communication tool as well, to help facilitate a workshop with users in the field, or to 
communicate the concept to the electrical and mechanical engineers before they develop functional prototypes.
P U R P O S E 
The Scenario method helps to foresee poor user 
experiences in a product before building a very 
expensive prototype. This lets a design team try out 
different versions, create a clear vision for the robot and 
actually try it out in context before it is build.
P R E P A R A T I O N S 
The preparation depends on at which level of detail the 
method is used. Initially the preparation consists of the 
Interaction Mapping and the Robot Personality from the 
previous methods. These should clearly define how the 
robot is acting in the played out scenarios. If the Robot 
Personality or the Interaction Mapping end up being 
unfitted for the task they should be updated so that 
everything is in sync. For the play out some mock-up 
tools should be available like cardboard, markers, tape 
and coloured paper, to make up design changes on the 
spot.
S T A G I N G 
The method builds on the general interaction overview 
from the Interaction Mapping method and the ideas 
and concepts from the Context of the Robot method 
earlier in the process. It is important for the staging 
that everybody is on their feet and participate in the 
actual brainstorming. The roles are to be taken seriously 
and the role of the robot needs to be building on the 
personality agreed upon in the previous methods. It is 
figure 6–14.	
SCENARIO CARD  TEMPLATE
==PAGE:95==
8 0   |  
important for all of the team to say all assumptions out 
loud in the play-through to make sure nothing is based 
on prejudices.
H O W  T O  U S E  T H E  M E T H O D 
Scenario is used by going into each of the predicted 
interactions and walking through the steps of the event. 
The roles of the user/-s are dealt amongst the design 
team, and more importantly the role of the robot. The 
person playing the robot should use a simple mock-up 
or cardboard box to simulate the robot’s presence, but 
control it’s movements and sounds. Another possibility 
is to play it in person, but make sure the face is 
covered, since the face provides a lot of unconscious 
social feedback, that would be overseen in the design 
of the real robot. Two bystanders are selected, one to 
write notes on the Scenario template and one to be 
a critic of the design (see the scenario card template 
in figure 6–14). The critic is to ask the hard questions; 
What are you thinking when the robot does that? 
What made you think to look there? And so on. This is 
simply to spike the discussion and make sure that all 
the alternatives have been considered. The note-taker 
uses the method template to take notes on the sound, 
motion and appearances of the interaction concepts 
and the users reaction to them. An example of a filled 
out template can be seen in figure 6–15. This is done 
with every new iteration of the concept. The left side 
of the template is regarded to the robots movements, 
sounds and appearances as well as any extra features 
that are needed for the robot to be able to interact 
like shown. The right side of the template is used to 
describe how the user experience was with the robot 
acting like it did. This can be important to come up with 
better solutions or simply as a discussion tool to make 
sure all reactions are thought through. After the run 
through of one iteration the team discusses changes 
and improvements and then runs through it one more 
time. When the team is confident in the solution the 
scenario can be run through with prospective users in a 
workshop to see whether further suggestions must be 
incorporated before building a prototype.
S U G G E S T E D  P R O C E D U R E
1.	
Team Leader chooses a scenario from the 
interaction map to run through
2.	
Team divides roles between them; robot, 
user/-s, note-taker and critic
3.	
The team member playing the robot uses a 
cardboard box or puts something over his/her 
face.
4.	
The team plays through the scenario with the 
initially predicted steps while the note-taker 
writes down what happens
5.	
The critic asks questions for clarification and 
the team discusses, what was on purpose and 
which moves where done based on assumptions
6.	
The team plays though the scenario again with 
the changes discussed
7.	
The team discusses again, what can be better 
and what was successful for the user
8.	
Step 6 and 7 are repeated until the team are 
confident in their solution
9.	
The process is repeated with the next scenario 
involving user interaction
	
...
n.	
The team prioritizes the 3 best scenarios to 
bring to a run through with the customers/users
T I P S ,  T R I C K S  A N D  C O N C E R N S 
The assumptions involved in interactions are very 
subtle, why the covering of the face is so important for 
8 0   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
==PAGE:96==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   8 1
the person playing the robot. Other restrictions, like 
making sure the arms do not move if the robot does 
not have arms, or that the person sits on a chair if the 
robot is shorter, are important too. This can completely 
change the way we view a product and interact with it.
As in the previous method sound apps could be useful. 
This is a place where alternatives like non-verbal 
communication or non-linguistic utterances could be 
tried out without consequences, so make the most of 
it. How could it sound without using natural language? 
Try to change pitch, timbre, frequency, duration, volume 
and rhythm to get a different emotion. Try to be inspired 
from animal sounds.
If the team is big other roles that could be used are: The 
defender of the concept, a bystander of the interaction, 
the apprentice (spørgejørgen) or the team can come up 
with their own.
There can also be a value in using the method 
alternatively with a Stop-Go approach to make sure all 
the questions are considered. This would work with 
allowing anyone to say ”Stop” during the play-through 
and the interaction pauses to make time for explanation 
of a certain action/reaction or a missing action/reaction. 
When the team is done discussing the team member 
says ”Go” and the team continues the interaction from 
just before the ”Stop”, incorporating the alteration. This 
should be done only if it doesn’t ruins the flow of the 
team and evaluations should still be done after the 
entire scenario has been played through.
O U T C O M E 
The outcome of this method is an overall understanding 
of how the robot should act in interaction with it’s 
user, which will influence the design, both regarding 
visual appearance, mechanical movements and sound 
design. This, together with the altered interaction map, 
is a direct input to the UX designers, as well as the 
electrical, mechanical and software engineers and can 
be used as a communication tool with the users and 
surrounding actors as a training piece to ensure easier 
domestication.
E X A M P L E 
In a workshop the design team tests the interaction 
of turning on the robot, taken from the Interaction 
Mapping. Here a store employee and the robot are 
the two only characters, so these are dealt to two 
from the team. A note taker and a critic is appointed 
as well. The team member playing the robot picks up 
a big cardboard box and moves it around to simulate 
the robots movements, keeping the Robot Personality 
in mind, while the employee character acts according 
to the persona described in the Interaction Mapping. 
First run through is done without interruptions while 
the remaining team members takes notes on how the 
robot is played and how the employee reacts to this. 
The person playing the robot is doing biiping sounds 
to simulate that it has been turned on and move 
forwared in a straight line. When the run-through is 
done the team discusses which improvements could 
be done and why the employee acted like he/she did. 
It is agreed that moving in a straight line becomes 
awkward and mechanistic and the team decides to 
make the movement more smooth. The note-taker 
makes sure to document the for and against arguments 
on the scenario card and the scenario is played through 
again with the new changes. An example of a filled out 
Scenario card can be seen in figure E.22.
L I M I T A T I O N S  A N D  A LT E R N A T I V E S 
The Scenario method has the same limitations as the 
Interaction Mapping method. If used internally in the 
design team there are some risks in basing design 
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   8 1
==PAGE:97==
82   |  
decisions on assumptions that might not hold true. 
To accommodate for this the method should never 
be used internally alone, but also as a pre-test and 
evaluation piece with users in context. If an alternative 
to the method is wanted some of the same insights 
can be obtained using ordinary roleplay or interaction 
prototyping as described by van Boeijen et al. (2014). 
Where there is a bigger budget and more time for the 
development process product evaluations with users 
(van Boeijen et al. 2014) can be useful as well as 3D 
animations of the product concept (see appendix A.2.3).

==PAGE:98==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   8 3
figure 6–15.	
SCENARIO CARD  TEMPLATE EXAMPLE
==PAGE:99==

6 . 3   S U G G E S T E D 
U S E F L O W
The useflow for the toolbox presented in this section 
is a suggested one. Alternatively the HRI Toolbox can 
be adapted to any current workflow and integrated in 
the development process where it fits. The useflow 
is centred around the methods of the toolbox, which 
are all used during workshops that gather the most 
relevant people for the task. The flow of events have 
been visualised in figure 6–16 and an example of it in 
use can be seen in figure 6–17. The process is expected 
to be an iterative one, where the useflow can be run 
through several times at different level of details. Many 
of the methods can besides be used both internally or 
in workshops with clients or users depending on the 
required outcome. When integrated into a generic Stage 
Gate Model the methods of the toolbox are all contained 
within the development stage of the process (see figure 
6–1). Before going into the useflow it is expected that an 
overall project scope and plan has been laid down, that 
a suitable business case has been chosen and technical 
and market opportunities identified to create a frame for 
the work on HRI design.
S C O P I N G 
A critical step to start off the useflow is an HRI scoping 
meeting including the entire design team, development 
team leaders and the project manager1. Here the group 
should agree upon an project scope and plan for the 
HRI design, as well as the expectations and visions 
for the final product. The design team leader can draft 
a proposal of the scope and plan to prepare for and 
initiate the meeting. During the meeting the values 
1  To clarify, the project manager is here defined as the person managing 
the overall development project of the robot, the development team is 
presumed to consist of an electronic, mechanic, software, field research 
and design team, each having a team leader. We define the design team 
as the one focusing on and responsible for developing the concept for 
the robotic product, the user experience and HRI design.
and outcome of each step of the process must be 
discussed and agreed upon, the workshops scheduled 
and relevant participants identified. The methods 
used in this phase can vary, but could include different 
prioritizing and planning tools, such as a Gant chart2 for 
instance.
E X P L O R A T I O N 
The next phase will include an exploration of the users 
and context in question. Here we suggest methods 
and tools such as interviews, observations, master 
and apprentice3 etc. The important thing is to gain 
an understanding of the intended users, context and 
market, as it is input needed when using the methods 
of the HRI Toolbox. To maintain knowledge gained 
during field studies, photos, videos and sound bits from 
context should be acquired as documentation.
A N A LY S I S 
In the analysis phase personas should be developed as 
input to a workshop revolving the Interaction Mapping 
method. The workshop builds on the knowledge 
from the exploration phase on the current scenario 
and the need of the users, and should include both 
field researchers and the design team to transfer the 
knowledge. The workshop should be facilitated by one 
of the participants to ensure efficient time management. 
When the workshop is over an overview of known 
events and interactions should have been produced 
as well as detailed flowcharts of what happens in at 
least the most important/common/critical interactions. 
Beyond the Interaction Mapping method the design 
team and field researchers can apply other analysis 
2  A Gant chart is a visual project planning tool. It is a bar chart showing 
the start and finish points of a project as well as the planned duration of 
the tasks involved (Gantt 1923).
3  Master and apprentice is a method where designers follow a user 
around in their work learning about their ways of doing things, like an 
apprentice learning from a master (Holtzblatt 1998).

SUGGESTED USEFLOW
==PAGE:101==
8 6   |  
methodology that they see fit for the project, such as 
Function-Means Tree4, Objectives Tree5, etc..
I D E A T I O N 
Building on the realizations of the robot’s interactions, 
users and context from the Interaction Mapping 
method, the team should arrange the next workshop 
fairly soon after. Here first the Brand Personality 
exercise should be worked through as well as the Robot 
Personality method.
The Brand Personality is meant as a staging exercise to 
remind the design team of the brand they are designing 
for and preparing them to think about inanimate objects 
as having a personality. When finished it should result in 
a characterization of the brand personality. If the robot 
designed should in no way reflect the brand profile, this 
exercise can be skipped. To prepare for the exercise 
the workshop participants should read what documents 
exist on the brand profile. This could be the mission 
and vision statement, values, etc.. The leadership 
responsible for the brand profile should be present for 
this part of the workshop as well as the design team 
to ensure consistency. One participant should facilitate 
to ensure a common understanding of the goal and 
continues progression with the exercise. The Brand 
Personality is a great exercise both for companies 
not yet very defined and for bigger companies having 
an extensive portfolio more or less in sync with the 
company profile. Other companies having a very clear 
brand definition will be able to go through the step in a 
few minutes.
4  A Function-Means Tree is an analysis method, where first the main 
function of a product is identified, then the means supporting that 
function, then the functions supporting those means etc. until a tree-like 
structure has mapped all functions and means (Cross 2008).
5  An Objectives Tree is an analysis method for identifying high and 
low level objectives with a product. A tree-like structure mappes the 
objectives that are related by asking how when going one level down 
and why, when going one level up (Cross 2008).
When the team is settled on the first task the Robot 
Personality method should be properly staged. This 
should take place in a creative environment that lets the 
team freely speak their mind, why only the design team 
should be present to ensure a free and creative flow 
without timidity or interruptions. Again one participant 
should facilitate the method to manage an efficient 
discussion. The Robot Personality method should result 
in a document describing the ideal personality of the 
robot to be designed. It is important to agree upon the 
premiss that the outcome is first iteration of the robotic 
personality. It is a dynamic document that can be 
changed with new realizations of the interactions, users 
and context.
To follow the Robot Personality other ideation methods 
can be utilized to come up with ideas and concepts for 
HRI design e.g. Brainstorming, Morphology, Bio-inspired 
design, etc.. The design team can apply these as they 
see fit, working individually or in groups.
An ideation method that should also be used in this 
phase is Context of the Robot. It revisits the material 
gathered in the exploration phase in an effort to create 
ideas and concepts for the HRI design fitting to the 
context of the robot. The method should be used in a 
workshop where the design team meets with the field 
researchers. One from the design team can in this 
workshop work as facilitators, while the field researchers 
will work as context ”experts”, preparing by assembling 
photos, video and sound clips from contextual visits 
relevant for the topic of the brainstorm. The method is 
worked through to include all dimensions and should 
result in a broad spectrum of ideas for the HRI design.
B U I L D I N G 
To follow up on the best ideas and concepts from the 
ideation phase, a building phase can take them to the 
8 6   |  T H E  H R I  D ES I G N  T O O L B OX
==PAGE:102==
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   87
next level. Here they can be made into pre-prototypes 
or mock-ups to get an increased understanding of 
function and construction. The mock-ups should not be 
too advanced at the early stages of development before 
a proof of concept has been achieved. The design team 
can use their own methods in this phase, but should at 
least produce a mock-up to be used in the evaluation 
phase.
E V A L U A T I O N 
The document created during the Robot Personality 
method can be used as an evaluation tool on the ideas 
an concepts produced. It should constantly be revisited 
to evaluate and further develop ideas. The design team 
can leave it hanging near their working area to keep it 
in mind when they ideate. They should evaluate their 
ideas by asking whether they reflect the personality 
decided upon, how they could reflect it further or if the 
personality needs adjustments.
To evaluate their best concepts the design team 
should also use the Scenario method regularly. Here 
the quality of the concepts are tested in the situations 
found in the initial Interaction Mapping. The method 
is used in workshops, where the design team and 
field researchers should be represented. One from the 
design team can facilitate the workshop, while the field 
researchers function as context and user ”experts” to 
inform the session. A field researcher could for instance 
take the role as the user to give a more realistic 
performance. The mock-up produced in the building 
phase can function as the robot character with help 
from a workshop participant. The staging of this step 
is important since role-play can be challenging to bring 
into a development process. It is important that this 
workshop takes place in a creative environment, where 
the participants feel free to speak their mind and act 
out the scenarios without feeling ridiculed. Including 
an icebreaker in the beginning of this workshop can 
be good way to obtain such an atmosphere. To keep 
focus in the work only one or a few scenarios should be 
processed in each workshop. As time is usually limited 
it is suggested to start out with the most important/
common/critical scenarios to be sure to get through 
those. The result of the method is an initial validation 
of the concepts. The method can also be utilized 
in a workshop with clients or users instead of field 
researchers to increase the validity of the results, but 
even then it might be advantageous to work through 
the scenarios internally first to mature the concepts, as 
the time with clients and users are often precious.
In addition to the Robot Personality and Scenario 
method the design team can also apply their own 
methods to evaluate the designs. This is especially 
relevant at later stages of development, when the 
concepts have matured and simple mock-ups and 
role-play can no longer provide the needed information. 
Then it might be relevant to introduce CAD drawings, 
animations, tele-operated or functioning prototypes, 
user tests, etc..
Running through the useflow once will often not be 
enough. The development process is an iterative one, 
where some or all of the steps will have to be revisited 
at different levels of detail. It can also be imagined that 
different features of a robot will be designed running 
through this process successively or in parallel. The 
number of iterations needed greatly depends on the 
ambitions and time frame of any project.
D E V E LO P M E N T  O F  T O O L B OX  C O N TA I N I N G  M E T H O D S  F O R  H R I  D E S I G N   |   87
